[Page 9] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 9] What is AWS Ground Station?
[Page 9] AWS Ground Station is a fully managed service that provides secure, fast, and predictable satellite
[Page 9] communications across a global infrastructure. With AWS Ground Station, you no longer have to
[Page 9] build, manage, or scale your own ground station infrastructure. AWS Ground Station enables you
[Page 9] to focus on innovating and rapidly experimenting with new applications that ingest satellite data,
[Page 9] rather than spend resources on building, operating, and scaling your own ground stations.
[Page 9] Using AWS's low-latency, high-bandwidth global fiber network, you can begin processing your
[Page 9] satellite data within seconds of reception at the antenna system. This enables you to turn raw data
[Page 9] into processed information or analyzed knowledge within a matter of seconds.
[Page 9] AWS Ground Station allows you to communicate with your satellites bi-directionally and supports
[Page 9] the following use cases:
[Page 9] • Downlink data – Receive data from your satellites, transmitting X-band and S-band frequencies,
[Page 9] delivered to an Amazon EC2 instance in real-time (VITA-49 format), or directly to an Amazon
[Page 9] S3 bucket in your account (PCAP format). Additionally, for satellites that use a supported
[Page 9] modulation and encoding scheme, you can choose between receiving data that is demodulated
[Page 9] and decoded, or the raw digital intermediate frequency (DigIF) samples (VITA-49 format).
[Page 9] • Uplink data – Send data and commands to your satellites, that receive S-band frequencies, by
[Page 9] sending DigIF data (VITA-49 format) to be transmitted by AWS Ground Station.
[Page 10] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 10] • Uplink echo – Validate commands sent to your spacecraft, and perform other advanced tasks, by
[Page 10] receiving your transmitted signal on a physically co-located antenna.
[Page 10] • Software Defined Radio (SDR) / Front End Processor (FEP) – Use your existing SDR and/or FEP,
[Page 10] that's capable of running on an Amazon EC2 instance, to process your data in real-time to send/
[Page 10] receive your existing waveforms, and generate your data products.
[Page 10] • Telemetry, Tracking, and Command (TT&C) – Perform TT&C using a combination of the
[Page 10] previously listed use cases to manage your satellite fleet.
[Page 10] • Cross Region Data Delivery – Operate multiple simultaneous contacts using AWS Ground
[Page 10] Station’s global antenna network from a single AWS Region.
[Page 10] • Digital twin – Test scheduling, verification of configurations, and proper error handling at a
[Page 10] reduced cost without using production antenna capacity.
[Page 10] We recommend that you begin by reading the following sections:
[Page 10] • To learn essential AWS Ground Station concepts, see How AWS Ground Station works.
[Page 10] • To learn how to set up your account and resources to use AWS Ground Station, see Get started.
[Page 10] • To programmatically use AWS Ground Station, please refer to the AWS Ground Station API
[Page 10] Reference. The API Reference describes all the API operations for AWS Ground Station in detail.
[Page 10] It also provides sample requests, responses, and errors for the supported web service protocols.
[Page 10] You can use the AWS CLI, or an AWS SDK, in the language of your choice, to write code that
[Page 10] interacts with AWS Ground Station.
[Page 11] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 11] How AWS Ground Station works
[Page 11] AWS Ground Station operates ground-based antennas to facilitate communication with your
[Page 11] satellite. The physical characteristics of what the antennas can do are abstracted and are referred
[Page 11] to as capabilities. The physical location of the antenna along with its current capabilities can be
[Page 11] referenced in the AWS Ground Station Locations section. Please contact us through the AWS
[Page 11] Support Center Console if your use case requires additional capabilities, additional location
[Page 11] offerings, or more precise antenna locations.
[Page 11] To use one of the AWS Ground Station antennas you must reserve a time at a specific location.
[Page 11] This reservation is referred to as a contact. To successfully schedule a contact, AWS Ground Station
[Page 11] requires additional data to ensure its success.
[Page 11] • Your satellite must be onboarded to one or more locations – This ensures you have approval to
[Page 11] operate the various capabilities at the requested location.
[Page 11] • Your satellite must have a valid ephemeris – This ensures the antennas have line of sight and
[Page 11] can accurately point at your satellite during the contact.
[Page 11] • You must have a valid mission profile – This allows you to customize how this contact will
[Page 11] behave including how you will receive and send data to your satellite. You may utilize multiple
[Page 11] mission profiles for the same vehicle to create different contacts to fit different operating
[Page 11] postures or scenarios you encounter.
[Page 11] Onboarding a satellite into AWS Ground Station is a multistep process involving data collection,
[Page 11] technical validation, spectrum licensing, with integration and testing. The Satellite onboarding
[Page 11] section of the guide will walk you through this process.
[Page 11] Mission profile composition
[Page 11] The satellite frequency information, data plane information, and other details are encapsulated
[Page 11] into a mission profile. The mission profile is a collection of config components. This allows you
[Page 11] to reuse config components across different mission profiles as suits your use case. Since mission
[Page 11] profiles don't directly reference individual satellites, but instead only have information about their
[Page 11] technical capabilities, mission profiles can also be reused by multiple satellites that have the same
[Page 11] Satellite onboarding 3
[Page 12] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 12] A valid mission profile will have a tracking config and one or more dataflows. The tracking config
[Page 12] will specify your preference for tracking during a contact. Each config pair within a dataflow
[Page 12] establishes a source and destination. Depending on your satellite and its operational modes, the
[Page 12] exact number of dataflows will vary in a mission profile to represent your uplink and downlink
[Page 12] communication paths as well as any data processing aspects.
[Page 12] • For more information on configuring your Amazon VPC, Amazon S3, and Amazon EC2 resources
[Page 12] that will be used during a contact, see Work with dataflows.
[Page 12] • For details on how each config behaves, see Use AWS Ground Station Configs.
[Page 12] • For specific details on all parameters expected, see Use AWS Ground Station Mission Profiles.
[Page 12] • For examples on how various mission profiles can be created to support your use case, see
[Page 12] Example mission profile configurations.
[Page 12] The following diagram shows an example mission profile and additional resources needed. Note
[Page 12] that the example shows a dataflow endpoint which is not needed for this mission profile, named
[Page 12] unusedEndpoint, to demonstrate the flexibility. The example supports the following dataflows:
[Page 12] • Synchronous downlink of digital intermediate frequency data to an Amazon EC2 instance that
[Page 12] you manage. Denoted by the name digIfDownlink.
[Page 12] • Asynchronous downlink of digital intermediate frequency data to an Amazon S3 bucket. Denoted
[Page 12] by the bucket name aws-groundstation-demo.
[Page 12] • Synchronous downlink of demodulated and decoded data to an Amazon EC2 instance that you
[Page 12] manage. Denoted by the name demodDecodeDownlink.
[Page 12] • Synchronous uplink of data from an Amazon EC2 instance that you manage to a AWS Ground
[Page 12] Station managed antenna. Denoted by the name digIfUplink.
[Page 12] Mission profile composition 4
[Page 13] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 13] With a valid mission profile, you can request a contact with your onboarded satellites. The contact
[Page 13] reservation request is asynchronous to allow time for the global antenna service to achieve a
[Page 13] consistent schedule across all AWS Regions involved. During this process, various antennas at the
[Page 13] requested ground station location are evaluated to determine if they are available and capable to
[Page 13] process the contact. During this process, your configured dataflow endpoints are also evaluated
[Page 13] to determine their availability. While this evaluation is occurring, the contact status will be in
[Page 13] This asynchronous scheduling process will finish within five minutes of the request, but typically
[Page 13] finishes within one minute. Please review Automate AWS Ground Station with Events for event-
[Page 13] based monitoring during scheduling time.
[Page 14] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 14] Contacts which can be performed and have availability result in SCHEDULED contacts. With a
[Page 14] scheduled contact, the resources which are needed to perform your contact have been reserved
[Page 14] across the needed AWS Regions as defined by your mission profile. Contacts which cannot
[Page 14] be performed, or have unavailable parts will result in FAILED_TO_SCHEDULE contacts. See
[Page 14] Troubleshoot FAILED_TO_SCHEDULE contacts for debugging details.
[Page 14] AWS Ground Station will automatically orchestrate your AWS managed resources during your
[Page 14] contact reservation. If applicable, you are responsible for orchestrating EC2 resources defined by
[Page 15] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 15] your mission profile as dataflow endpoints. AWS Ground Station provides AWS EventBridge Events
[Page 15] for automating orchestration of your resources to reduce costs. See Automate AWS Ground Station
[Page 15] with Events for more details.
[Page 15] During the contact, telemetry about your contact performance is delivered to AWS CloudWatch.
[Page 15] For information about how to monitor your contact during execution, please see Understand
[Page 15] monitoring with AWS Ground Station.
[Page 15] The following diagram continues the previous example by showing the same resources
[Page 15] orchestrated during the contact.
[Page 15] Not all the antenna capabilities were used in this example. For instance, there are more
[Page 15] than a dozen antenna downlink capabilities available at each antenna that support multiple
[Page 15] frequencies and polarizations. For more details about the number of each capability
[Page 15] type available from AWS Ground Station antennas, and their supported frequencies and
[Page 15] polarizations, see AWS Ground Station Site Capabilities.
[Page 16] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 16] At the end of your contact, AWS Ground Station will assess the performance of your contact
[Page 16] and will determine a final contact status. Contacts where no errors are detected will result in a
[Page 16] COMPLETED contact status. Contacts where service errors have caused data delivery issues during
[Page 16] the contact will result in an AWS_FAILED status. Contacts where client or user errors have caused
[Page 16] data delivery issues during the contact will result in a FAILED status. Errors outside a contact time,
[Page 16] that is during pre-pass or post-pass, are not taken into account during the adjudication.
[Page 16] See Understand contact lifecycle for more information.
[Page 17] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 17] The digital twin feature for AWS Ground Station allows you to schedule contacts against virtual
[Page 17] ground station locations. These virtual ground stations are exact replicas of production ground
[Page 17] stations including antenna capabilities, site masks, and actual GPS coordinates. The digital twin
[Page 17] feature enables you to test your contact orchestration workflow for a fraction of the cost compared
[Page 17] to production ground stations. See Use the AWS Ground Station digital twin feature for more
[Page 17] Understand AWS Ground Station Core components
[Page 17] This section provides detailed definitions for the core components of AWS Ground Station.
[Page 17] The following diagram shows the core components of AWS Ground Station and how they relate
[Page 17] to each other. The arrows indicate the direction of the dependencies between components, where
[Page 17] each component points to its dependencies.
[Page 18] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 18] Understand AWS Ground Station Core components 10
[Page 19] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 19] The following topics describe the AWS Ground Station core components in detail.
[Page 19] • Use AWS Ground Station Mission Profiles
[Page 19] • Use AWS Ground Station Configs
[Page 19] • Use AWS Ground Station Dataflow endpoint groups
[Page 19] • Use AWS Ground Station Agent
[Page 19] Use AWS Ground Station Mission Profiles
[Page 19] Mission profiles contain configs and parameters for how contacts are executed. When you reserve
[Page 19] a contact or search for available contacts, you supply the mission profile that you intend to use.
[Page 19] Mission profiles bring all of your configs together and define how the antenna will be configured
[Page 19] and where data will go during your contact.
[Page 19] Mission profiles can be shared across satellites which share the same radio characteristics. You can
[Page 19] create additional dataflow endpoint groups to bound the maximum simultaneous contacts you
[Page 19] want to perform for your constellation.
[Page 19] Tracking configs are specified as a unique field within the mission profile. Tracking configs are used
[Page 19] to specify your preference for using program-tracking and auto-tracking during your contact. For
[Page 19] more information, see Tracking Config.
[Page 19] All other configs are contained in the dataflowEdges field of the mission profile. These configs
[Page 19] can be thought of as dataflow nodes that each represent an AWS Ground Station managed
[Page 19] resource that can send or receive data and its associated configuration. The dataflowEdges field
[Page 19] defines which source and destination dataflow nodes (configs) are needed. A single dataflow edge
[Page 19] is a list of two config Amazon Resource Names (ARNs)—the first is the source config and the second
[Page 19] is the destination config. By specifying a dataflow edge between two configs, you are telling AWS
[Page 19] Ground Station from where and to where data should flow during a contact. For more information,
[Page 19] see Use AWS Ground Station Configs.
[Page 19] The contactPrePassDurationSeconds and contactPostPassDurationSeconds allow you
[Page 19] to specify times relative to the contact where you will receive an CloudWatch Event notification.
[Page 19] For a timeline of events related to your contact, please read Understand contact lifecycle.
[Page 19] The name field of the mission profile helps distinguish between the mission profiles that you
[Page 20] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 20] The streamsKmsRole and streamsKmsKey are used to define the encryption used by AWS
[Page 20] Ground Station for your data delivery with AWS Ground Station Agent. Please see Data encryption
[Page 20] during transit for AWS Ground Station.

[Page 22] A full list of parameters and examples is included at the following documentation.
[Page 22] • AWS::GroundStation::MissionProfile CloudFormation resource type
[Page 22] Use AWS Ground Station Configs
[Page 22] Configs are resources that AWS Ground Station uses to define the parameters for each aspect of
[Page 22] your contact. Add the configs you want to a mission profile, and then that mission profile will be
[Page 22] used when executing the contact. You can define several different types of configs. The configs can
[Page 22] be grouped into two categories:
[Page 22] A TrackingConfig is the only type of tracking config. It's used to configure the autotrack setting of
[Page 22] the antenna during a contact, and is required in a mission profile.
[Page 22] The configs that can be used in a mission profile dataflow can be thought of as dataflow nodes
[Page 22] that each represent an AWS Ground Station managed resource that can send or receive data. A
[Page 22] mission profile requires at least one pair of these configs, with one representing a source of data,
[Page 22] and one representing a destination. These configs are summarized in the following table.
[Page 22] Config name Dataflow source/destination
[Page 22] AntennaDownlinkConfig Source
[Page 22] AntennaDownlinkDemodDecodeConfig Source
[Page 22] UplinkEchoConfig Source
[Page 22] S3RecordingConfig Destination
[Page 22] AntennaUplinkConfig Destination
[Page 22] DataflowEndpointConfig Source and/or Destination
[Page 23] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 23] See the following documentation for more information about how to perform operations on
[Page 23] configs using CloudFormation, the AWS Command Line Interface, or the AWS Ground Station API.
[Page 23] Links to documentation for specific config types are also provided below.
[Page 23] • AWS::GroundStation::Config CloudFormation resource type
[Page 23] • Config AWS CLI reference
[Page 23] • Config API reference
[Page 23] You can use tracking configs in the mission profile to determine whether autotrack should be
[Page 23] enabled during your contacts. This config has a single parameter: autotrack. The autotrack
[Page 23] parameter can have the following values:
[Page 23] • REQUIRED - Autotrack is required for your contacts.
[Page 23] • PREFERRED - Autotrack is preferred for contacts, but contacts can still be executed without
[Page 23] • REMOVED - No autotrack should be used for your contacts.
[Page 23] AWS Ground Station will utilize programmatic tracking which will point based on your ephemeris
[Page 23] when autotrack is not used. Please reference Understand how AWS Ground Station uses
[Page 23] ephemerides for details about how ephemeris is constructed.
[Page 23] Autotrack will use program tracking until the expected signal is found. Once that occurs, it will
[Page 23] continue to track based on the strength of the signal.
[Page 23] See the following documentation for more information about how to perform operations on
[Page 23] tracking configs using CloudFormation, the AWS Command Line Interface, or the AWS Ground
[Page 23] • AWS::GroundStation::Config TrackingConfig CloudFormation property
[Page 23] • Config AWS CLI reference (see the trackingConfig -> (structure) section)
[Page 23] • TrackingConfig API reference
[Page 24] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 24] Antenna Downlink Config
[Page 24] You can use antenna downlink configs to configure the antenna for downlink during your contact.
[Page 24] They consist of a spectrum config that specifies the frequency, bandwidth, and polarization that
[Page 24] should be used during your downlink contact.
[Page 24] This config represents a source node in a dataflow. It is responsible for digitizing radio frequency
[Page 24] data. Data streamed from this node will follow the Signal Data/IP Format. For more detailed
[Page 24] information on how to construct dataflows with this config, see Work with dataflows
[Page 24] If your downlink use case requires demodulation or decoding, see the Antenna Downlink Demod
[Page 24] See the following documentation for more information about how to perform operations on
[Page 24] antenna downlink configs using CloudFormation, the AWS Command Line Interface, or the AWS
[Page 24] • AWS::GroundStation::Config AntennaDownlinkConfig CloudFormation property
[Page 24] • Config AWS CLI reference (see the antennaDownlinkConfig -> (structure) section)
[Page 24] • AntennaDownlinkConfig API reference
[Page 24] Antenna Downlink Demod Decode Config
[Page 24] Antenna downlink demod decode configs are a more complex and customizable config type that
[Page 24] you can use to execute downlink contacts with demodulation and/or decoding. If you're interested
[Page 24] in executing these types of contacts, please open an AWS Support ticket through the AWS Support
[Page 24] Center Console. We'll help you define the right config and mission profile for your use case.
[Page 24] This config represents a source node in a dataflow. It is responsible for digitizing radio frequency
[Page 24] data and performing the demodulation and decoding as specified. Data streamed from this node
[Page 24] will follow the Demodulated/Decoded Data/IP Format. For more detailed information on how to
[Page 24] construct dataflows with this config, see Work with dataflows
[Page 24] See the following documentation for more information about how to perform operations on
[Page 24] antenna downlink demod decode configs using CloudFormation, the AWS Command Line Interface,
[Page 24] or the AWS Ground Station API.

[Page 25] Antenna Uplink Config
[Page 25] You can use antenna uplink configs to configure the antenna for uplink during your contact. They
[Page 25] consist of a spectrum config with frequency, polarization, and target effective isotropic radiated
[Page 25] power (EIRP). For information about how to configure a contact for uplink loopback, see Antenna
[Page 25] This config represents a destination node in a dataflow. It will convert the provided digitized radio
[Page 25] frequency data signal into an analog signal and emit it for your satellite to receive. Data streamed
[Page 25] to this node is expected to meet the Signal Data/IP Format. For more detailed information on how
[Page 25] to construct dataflows with this config, see Work with dataflows
[Page 25] See the following documentation for more information about how to perform operations on
[Page 25] antenna uplink configs using CloudFormation, the AWS Command Line Interface, or the AWS
[Page 25] • AWS::GroundStation::Config AntennaUplinkConfig CloudFormation property
[Page 25] • Config AWS CLI reference (see the antennaUplinkConfig -> (structure) section)
[Page 25] • AntennaUplinkConfig API reference
[Page 25] Antenna Uplink Echo Config
[Page 25] Uplink echo configs tell the antenna how to execute an uplink echo. An uplink echo can be used
[Page 25] to validate commands sent to your spacecraft, and perform other advanced tasks. This is achieved
[Page 25] by recording the actual signal transmitted by the AWS Ground Station antenna (i.e. the uplink).
[Page 25] This echoes the signal sent by the antenna back to your dataflow endpoint and should match the
[Page 25] transmitted signal. An uplink echo config contains the ARN of an uplink config. The antenna uses
[Page 25] the parameters from the uplink config pointed to by the ARN when executing an uplink echo.
[Page 25] This config represents a source node in a dataflow. Data streamed from this node will meet the
[Page 25] Signal Data/IP Format. For more detailed information on how to construct dataflows with this
[Page 25] config, see Work with dataflows
[Page 26] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 26] See the following documentation for more information about how to perform operations on uplink
[Page 26] echo configs using CloudFormation, the AWS Command Line Interface, or the AWS Ground Station
[Page 26] • AWS::GroundStation::Config UplinkEchoConfig CloudFormation property
[Page 26] • Config AWS CLI reference (see the uplinkEchoConfig -> (structure) section)
[Page 26] • UplinkEchoConfig API reference
[Page 26] Dataflow Endpoint Config
[Page 26] Dataflow endpoint configs are only used for data delivery to Amazon EC2 and are not used
[Page 26] for data delivery to Amazon S3.
[Page 26] You can use dataflow endpoint configs to specify which dataflow endpoint in a dataflow endpoint
[Page 26] group from which or to which you want data to flow during a contact. The two parameters of a
[Page 26] dataflow endpoint config specify the name and region of the dataflow endpoint. When reserving
[Page 26] a contact, AWS Ground Station analyzes the mission profile you specified and attempts to find
[Page 26] a dataflow endpoint group within the AWS Region that contains all of the dataflow endpoints
[Page 26] specified by the dataflow endpoint configs contained in your mission profile. If a suitable dataflow
[Page 26] endpoint group is found, the contact status will become SCHEDULED, otherwise it will become
[Page 26] FAILED_TO_SCHEDULE. For more information about the possible statuses of a contact, see AWS
[Page 26] Ground Station contact statuses.
[Page 26] The dataflowEndpointName property of a dataflow endpoint config specifies which dataflow
[Page 26] endpoint in a dataflow endpoint group to which or from which data will flow during a contact.
[Page 26] The dataflowEndpointRegion property specifies which region the dataflow endpoint resides in.
[Page 26] If a region is specified in your dataflow endpoint config, AWS Ground Station looks for a dataflow
[Page 26] endpoint in the region specified. If no region is specified, AWS Ground Station will default to the
[Page 26] contact's ground station region. A contact is considered a cross region data delivery contact if your
[Page 26] dataflow endpoint's region is not the same as the contact's ground station region. See Work with
[Page 26] dataflows for more information on cross-region dataflows.
[Page 26] See Use AWS Ground Station Dataflow endpoint groups for tips on how different naming schemes
[Page 26] for your dataflows can benefit your use case.
[Page 27] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 27] For more detailed information on how to construct dataflows with this config, see Work with
[Page 27] See the following documentation for more information about how to perform operations on
[Page 27] dataflow endpoint configs using CloudFormation, the AWS Command Line Interface, or the AWS

[Page 27] • Config AWS CLI reference (see the dataflowEndpointConfig -> (structure) section)
[Page 27] • DataflowEndpointConfig API reference
[Page 27] Amazon S3 Recording Config
[Page 27] Amazon S3 recording configs are only used for data delivery to Amazon S3 and are not
[Page 27] used for data delivery to Amazon EC2.
[Page 27] This config represents a destination node in a dataflow. This node will encapsulate incoming data
[Page 27] from the dataflow's source node into pcap data. For more detailed information on how to construct
[Page 27] dataflows with this config, see Work with dataflows
[Page 27] You can use S3 recording configs to specify an Amazon S3 bucket to which you want downlinked
[Page 27] data delivered along with the naming convention used. The following specifies restrictions and
[Page 27] details about these parameters:
[Page 27] • The Amazon S3 bucket's name must begin with aws-groundstation .
[Page 27] • The IAM role must have a trust policy that allows the groundstation.amazonaws.com service
[Page 27] principal to assume the role. See the Example Trust Policy section below for an example. During
[Page 27] config creation the config resource id does not exist, the trust policy must use an asterisk (*) in
[Page 27] place of your-config-id and can be updated after creation with the config resource id.
[Page 27] For more information on how to update a role's trust policy, see Managing IAM roles in the IAM

[Page 30] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 30] Use AWS Ground Station Dataflow endpoint groups
[Page 30] Dataflow endpoints define the location where you want the data to be synchronously streamed
[Page 30] to or from during contacts. Dataflow endpoints are always created as part of a dataflow endpoint
[Page 30] group. By including multiple dataflow endpoints in a group, you are asserting that the specified
[Page 30] endpoints can all be used together during a single contact. For example, if a contact needs to send
[Page 30] data to three separate dataflow endpoints, you must have three endpoints in a single dataflow
[Page 30] endpoint group that match the dataflow endpoint configs in your mission profile.
[Page 30] Dataflow endpoint group versions
[Page 30] AWS Ground Station supports two versions of dataflow endpoint groups:
[Page 30] • DataflowEndpointGroup - The original implementation that supports uplink and downlink using
[Page 30] a dataflow endpoint, and downlink-only for an AWS Ground Station Agent endpoint
[Page 30] • DataflowEndpointGroupV2 - Updated version that supports both uplink and downlink
[Page 30] dataflows for AWS Ground Station Agent endpoints with improved clarity and functionality
[Page 30] Dataflow endpoint group comparison
[Page 30] Feature DataflowEndpointGroup DataflowEndpointGroupV2
[Page 30] Supported endpoint types DataflowEndpoint, DownlinkAwsGroundS
[Page 30] AwsGroundStationAg tationAgentEndpoint,
[Page 30] entEndpoint UplinkAwsGroundSta
[Page 30] Endpoints supporting uplink DataflowEndpoint UplinkAwsGroundSta
[Page 30] Endpoints supporting DataflowEndpoint, DownlinkAwsGroundS
[Page 30] downlink AwsGroundStationAg tationAgentEndpoint
[Page 30] DataflowEndpointGroupV2 was created to support uplink dataflows and to make
[Page 30] the language surrounding dataflow endpoint groups clearer. We recommend using
[Page 30] UplinkAwsGroundStationAgentEndpoint and DownlinkAwsGroundStationAgentEndpoint
[Page 30] endpoints with a DataflowEndpointGroupV2 for all new use cases. DataflowEndpointGroup
[Page 30] Dataflow endpoint groups 22
[Page 31] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 31] remains supported for backward compatibility, but DataflowEndpointGroupV2 provides enhanced
[Page 31] functionality and clearer configuration options.
[Page 31] The dataflow endpoints are identified by a name of your choosing when executing
[Page 31] contacts. These names do not need to be unique across the account. This allows multiple
[Page 31] contacts across different satellites and antenna to be executed at the same time using the
[Page 31] same mission profile. This can be useful if you have a constellation of satellites that have
[Page 31] the same operating characteristics. You can scale the number of dataflow endpoint groups
[Page 31] up to fit the maximum number of simultaneous contacts your constellation of satellite
[Page 31] When one or more resources in a dataflow endpoint group is in use for a contact, the entire group
[Page 31] is reserved for the duration of that contact. You may execute multiple contacts concurrently, but
[Page 31] those contacts must be executed on different dataflow endpoint groups.
[Page 31] Dataflow endpoint groups must be in a HEALTHY state to schedule contacts using them.
[Page 31] For information on how to troubleshoot dataflow endpoint groups that are not in a
[Page 31] HEALTHY state, see Troubleshoot DataflowEndpointGroups not in a HEALTHY state .
[Page 31] See the following documentation for more information about how to perform operations on
[Page 31] dataflow endpoint groups using CloudFormation, the AWS Command Line Interface, or the AWS
[Page 31] • AWS::GroundStation::DataflowEndpointGroup CloudFormation resource type
[Page 31] • Dataflow Endpoint Group AWS CLI reference
[Page 31] • Dataflow Endpoint Group API reference
[Page 31] The members of a dataflow endpoint group are dataflow endpoints. The supported endpoint types
[Page 31] depend on which dataflow endpoint group version you use.
[Page 31] Dataflow endpoint groups 23
[Page 32] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 32] DataflowEndpointGroup endpoints
[Page 32] DataflowEndpointGroup supports uplink and downlink using a dataflow endpoint, and downlink-
[Page 32] only for an AWS Ground Station Agent endpoint. For both types of endpoints, you will create the
[Page 32] supporting constructs (e.g. IP addresses) prior to creating the dataflow endpoint group. Please see
[Page 32] Work with dataflows for recommendations on which dataflow endpoint type to use and how to set
[Page 32] up the supporting constructs.
[Page 32] The following sections describe both supported endpoint types.
[Page 32] All dataflow endpoints within a single dataflow endpoint group must be of the same type.
[Page 32] You cannot mix AWS Ground Station Agent endpoints with Dataflow endpoints in the same
[Page 32] group. If your use case requires both types of endpoints, you must create separate dataflow
[Page 32] endpoint groups for each type.
[Page 32] For DataflowEndpointGroupV2, you can mix UplinkAwsGroundStationAgentEndpoint and
[Page 32] DownlinkAwsGroundStationAgentEndpoint in the same group.
[Page 32] AWS Ground Station Agent endpoint
[Page 32] The AWS Ground Station Agent Endpoint utilizes the AWS Ground Station Agent as a software
[Page 32] component to terminate connections. To construct an AWS Ground Station Agent Endpoint, you
[Page 32] will only populate the AwsGroundStationAgentEndpoint field of the EndpointDetails. For more
[Page 32] information about the AWS Ground Station Agent, see the full AWS Ground Station Agent User
[Page 32] The AwsGroundStationAgentEndpoint consists of the following:
[Page 32] • Name - The dataflow endpoint name. For the contact to use this dataflow endpoint, this name
[Page 32] must match the name used in your dataflow endpoint config.
[Page 32] • EgressAddress - The IP and port address used to egress data from the Agent.
[Page 32] • IngressAddress - The IP and port address used to ingress data to the Agent.
[Page 32] The Dataflow Endpoint utilizes a networking application as a software component to terminate
[Page 32] connections. Use Dataflow Endpoint when you want to uplink Digital Signal Data, downlink less-
[Page 32] Dataflow endpoint groups 24
[Page 33] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 33] than 50MHz of Digital Signal Data, or downlink Demodulated/Decoded Signal Data. To construct
[Page 33] a Dataflow Endpoint, you will populate the Endpoint and Security Details fields of the
[Page 33] The Endpoint consists of the following:
[Page 33] • Name - The dataflow endpoint name. For the contact to use this dataflow endpoint, this name
[Page 33] must match the name used in your dataflow endpoint config.
[Page 33] • Address - The IP and port address used.
[Page 33] The SecurityDetails consists of the following:
[Page 33] • roleArn - The Amazon Resource Name (ARN) of a role that AWS Ground Station will assume to
[Page 33] create Elastic Network Interfaces (ENIs) in your VPC. These ENIs serve as the ingress and egress
[Page 33] points of data streamed during a contact.
[Page 33] • securityGroupIds - The security groups to attach to the elastic network interfaces.
[Page 33] • subnetIds - A list of subnets where AWS Ground Station may place elastic network interfaces
[Page 33] to send streams to your instances. If multiple subnets are specified, they must be routable to
[Page 33] one another. If the subnets are in different Availability Zones (AZs), you may incur cross-AZ data
[Page 33] The IAM role passed into roleArn must have a trust policy that allows the
[Page 33] groundstation.amazonaws.com service principal to assume the role. See the Example Trust
[Page 33] Policy section below for an example. During endpoint creation the endpoint resource id does not
[Page 33] exist, so the trust policy must use an asterisk (*) in place of your-endpoint-id. This can be
[Page 33] updated after creation to use the endpoint resource id in order to scope the trust policy to that
[Page 33] specific dataflow endpoint group.
[Page 33] The IAM role must have an IAM policy that allows AWS Ground Station to set up the ENIs. See the
[Page 33] Example Role Policy section below for an example.
[Page 33] For more information on how to update a role's trust policy, see Managing IAM roles in the IAM

[Page 35] DataflowEndpointGroupV2 endpoints
[Page 35] DataflowEndpointGroupV2 introduces specialized endpoint types that provide clearer
[Page 35] configuration and enhanced functionality:
[Page 35] • UplinkAwsGroundStationAgentEndpoint - Optimized for uplink dataflows
[Page 35] • DownlinkAwsGroundStationAgentEndpoint - Optimized for downlink dataflows
[Page 35] These specialized endpoints replace the generic AwsGroundStationAgentEndpoint with direction-
[Page 35] specific configurations that make it easier to set up and manage your dataflows.
[Page 35] Uplink AWS Ground Station Agent endpoint
[Page 35] The UplinkAwsGroundStationAgentEndpoint is specifically designed for uplink dataflows and
[Page 35] provides clearer configuration options. Use this endpoint type when you need to provide data to
[Page 35] AWS Ground Station to be uplinked to your satellite.
[Page 35] The UplinkAwsGroundStationAgentEndpoint consists of the following:
[Page 35] • Name - The dataflow endpoint name. For the contact to use this dataflow endpoint, this name
[Page 35] must match the name used in your dataflow endpoint config.
[Page 35] • IngressAddressAndPort - Single IP and port address for data input to the agent
[Page 35] • AgentIpAndPortAddress - Port range for agent communication
[Page 35] Downlink AWS Ground Station Agent endpoint
[Page 35] The DownlinkAwsGroundStationAgentEndpoint is optimized for downlink dataflows, including
[Page 35] narrowband downlink, wideband demodulation/decode, and uplink echo scenarios.
[Page 35] Dataflow endpoint groups 27
[Page 36] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 36] The DownlinkAwsGroundStationAgentEndpoint consists of the following:
[Page 36] • Name - The dataflow endpoint name. For the contact to use this dataflow endpoint, this name
[Page 36] must match the name used in your dataflow endpoint config.
[Page 36] • EgressAddressAndPort - Single IP and port address for data output from the agent
[Page 36] • AgentIpAndPortAddress - Port range for agent communication
[Page 36] Creating dataflow endpoint groups
[Page 36] You can create dataflow endpoint groups using either version:
[Page 36] CreateDataflowEndpointGroup
[Page 36] Use CreateDataflowEndpointGroup for backward compatibility or when you need to use the
[Page 36] generic AwsGroundStationAgentEndpoint or DataflowEndpoint types.
[Page 36] CreateDataflowEndpointGroupV2
[Page 36] Use CreateDataflowEndpointGroupV2 for new implementations to take advantage of specialized
[Page 36] endpoint types that support both uplink and downlink dataflows. This API only supports
[Page 36] UplinkAwsGroundStationAgentEndpoint and DownlinkAwsGroundStationAgentEndpoint.
[Page 36] Migration considerations
[Page 36] If you're currently using DataflowEndpointGroup, you can continue using your existing
[Page 36] configuration without changes. AWS Ground Station maintains full backward compatibility.
[Page 36] If you'd like to migrate to use the new DataflowEndpointGroupV2, and are currently using a
[Page 36] DataflowEndpoint with a Dataflow Endpoint Application to receive your data, you'll need to
[Page 36] migrate to use the AWS Ground Station Agent instead. If you're already using an AWS Ground
[Page 36] Station Agent for downlink, you can use the same agent instance for uplink as well - no additional
[Page 36] agent instances are required.
[Page 36] To migrate to DataflowEndpointGroupV2:
[Page 36] 1. If migrating from DataflowEndpoint, set up the AWS Ground Station Agent following the AWS
[Page 36] Ground Station Agent User Guide
[Page 36] 2. Identify your dataflow direction and create the appropriate endpoint type
[Page 36] (UplinkAwsGroundStationAgentEndpoint or DownlinkAwsGroundStationAgentEndpoint)
[Page 36] Dataflow endpoint groups 28
[Page 37] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 37] 3. Create the DataflowEndpointGroupV2 referencing those endpoints
[Page 37] 4. Create a new dataflow endpoint config that references the new DataflowEndpointGroupV2 by
[Page 37] 5. Create a new mission profile that references the dataflow endpoint config as a dataflow edge
[Page 37] 6. Use the new mission profile to schedule contacts
[Page 37] 7. Test your configuration before deploying to production
[Page 37] For more information about the complete workflow, see Understand AWS Ground Station Core
[Page 37] components and Create configs.
[Page 37] Use AWS Ground Station Agent
[Page 37] The AWS Ground Station Agent enables you to receive (downlink) synchronous Wideband Digital
[Page 37] Intermediate Frequency (DigIF) dataflows during AWS Ground Station contacts.
[Page 37] You can select two options for data delivery:
[Page 37] 1. Data delivery to an EC2 instance - Data delivery to an EC2 instance that you own. You
[Page 37] manage the AWS Ground Station Agent. This option may suit you best if you need near real-
[Page 37] time data processing. See the Work with dataflows section for information about EC2 data
[Page 37] 2. Data delivery to an S3 bucket - Data delivery to your AWS S3 bucket is fully managed by AWS
[Page 37] Ground Station. See the Get started guide for information about S3 data delivery.
[Page 37] Both modes of data delivery require you to create a set of AWS resources. The use of
[Page 37] CloudFormation to create your AWS resources is highly recommended to ensure reliability,
[Page 37] accuracy, and supportability. Each contact can only deliver data to EC2 or S3 but not to both
[Page 37] The following diagram shows a DigIF dataflow from an AWS Ground Station Antenna Region to
[Page 37] your EC2 instance with your Software-Defined Radio (SDR) or similar listener.

[Page 39] Below are the best practices for AWS Identity and Access Management (IAM) and what permissions
[Page 39] you will need. After setting up the appropriate roles you can begin following the remainder of the
[Page 39] Sign up for an AWS account
[Page 39] If you do not have an AWS account, complete the following steps to create one.
[Page 39] To sign up for an AWS account
[Page 39] 1. Open https://portal.aws.amazon.com/billing/signup.
[Page 39] 2. Follow the online instructions.
[Page 39] Part of the sign-up procedure involves receiving a phone call or text message and entering a
[Page 39] verification code on the phone keypad.
[Page 39] When you sign up for an AWS account, an AWS account root user is created. The root user
[Page 39] has access to all AWS services and resources in the account. As a security best practice, assign
[Page 39] administrative access to a user, and use only the root user to perform tasks that require root
[Page 39] AWS sends you a confirmation email after the sign-up process is complete. At any time, you can
[Page 39] view your current account activity and manage your account by going to https://aws.amazon.com/
[Page 39] and choosing My Account.
[Page 39] Create a user with administrative access
[Page 39] After you sign up for an AWS account, secure your AWS account root user, enable AWS IAM Identity
[Page 39] Center, and create an administrative user so that you don't use the root user for everyday tasks.
[Page 39] Sign up for an AWS account 31
[Page 40] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 40] Secure your AWS account root user

[Page 40] entering your AWS account email address. On the next page, enter your password.
[Page 40] For help signing in by using root user, see Signing in as the root user in the AWS Sign-In User
[Page 40] 2. Turn on multi-factor authentication (MFA) for your root user.
[Page 40] For instructions, see Enable a virtual MFA device for your AWS account root user (console) in

[Page 40] the AWS Sign-In User Guide.

[Page 41] Add AWS Ground Station permissions to your AWS account
[Page 41] To use AWS Ground Station without requiring an administrative user, you need to create a new
[Page 41] policy and attach it to your AWS account.
[Page 41] 1. Sign in to the AWS Management Console and open the IAM console.
[Page 41] 2. Create a new policy. Use the following steps:
[Page 41] a. In the navigation pane, choose Policies and then choose Create Policy.
[Page 41] b. In the JSON tab, edit the JSON with one of the following values. Use the JSON that works
[Page 41] best for your application.
[Page 41] • For Ground Station administrative privileges, set Action to groundstation:* as follows:
[Page 41] "Version":"2012-10-17",
[Page 41] • For Read-only privileges, set Action to groundstation:Get*, groundstation:List*, and
[Page 41] groundstation:Describe* as follows:
[Page 41] Add AWS Ground Station permissions to your AWS account 33

[Page 42] "Version":"2012-10-17",

[Page 42] "groundstation:List*",
[Page 42] "groundstation:Describe*"

[Page 42] groundstation:*, and Condition/Bool to aws:MultiFactorAuthPresent:true as follows:

[Page 43] Onboarding a satellite into AWS Ground Station is a multistep process involving data collection,
[Page 43] technical validation, spectrum licensing, with integration and testing. There are also non-disclosure
[Page 43] agreements (NDAs) required.
[Page 43] Customer onboarding process overview
[Page 43] Satellite onboarding is a manual process that can be found on the Satellites and Resources section
[Page 43] of the AWS Ground Station console page. The following describes the overall process.
[Page 43] 1. Review the AWS Ground Station Locations section to determine if your satellite meets the
[Page 43] geographical and radio frequency characteristics.
[Page 43] 2. To start onboarding your satellite to AWS Ground Station, please submit a Satellite
[Page 43] Onboarding Questionnaire on the Satellites and Resources section of the AWS Ground Station
[Page 43] console page. Please include a brief summary of your mission and satellite needs, including
[Page 43] your organization name, the frequencies required, when the satellites will be or were launched,
[Page 43] the satellite's orbit type, and if you plan to use Use the AWS Ground Station digital twin
[Page 43] 3. Once your request is reviewed and approved, AWS Ground Station will apply for regulatory
[Page 43] licensing at the specific locations you plan to use. The duration of this step will vary depending
[Page 43] on the locations and any existing regulations.
[Page 43] 4. After this approval is obtained, your satellite will be visible for you to use. AWS Ground Station
[Page 43] will send you a notification of the successful update.
[Page 43] (Optional) Naming satellites
[Page 43] After onboarding, you may want to add a name to your satellite record to more easily recognize it.
[Page 43] The AWS Ground Station console has the ability to display a user defined name for a satellite along
[Page 43] with the Norad ID when using the Contacts page. Displaying the satellite name makes it much
[Page 43] easier to select the correct satellite when scheduling. To do this, tags can be used.
[Page 43] Tagging AWS Ground Station Satellites can be done via the tag-resource API with the AWS CLI
[Page 43] or one of the AWS SDKs. This guide will cover using the AWS Ground Station CLI to tag the public
[Page 43] broadcast satellite Aqua (Norad ID 27424) in us-west-2.
[Page 44] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 44] AWS Ground Station CLI
[Page 44] The AWS CLI can be used to interact with AWS Ground Station. Before using AWS CLI to tag your
[Page 44] satellites, the following AWS CLI prerequisites must be fulfilled:
[Page 44] • Ensure that AWS CLI is installed. For information about installing AWS CLI, see Installing the AWS
[Page 44] • Ensure that AWS CLI is configured. For information about configuring AWS CLI, see Configuring
[Page 44] the AWS CLI version 2.
[Page 44] • Save your frequently used configuration settings and credentials in files that are maintained by
[Page 44] the AWS CLI. You need these settings and credentials to reserve and manage your AWS Ground
[Page 44] Station contacts with AWS CLI. For more information about saving your configuration and
[Page 44] credential settings, see Configuration and credential file settings .
[Page 44] Once AWS CLI is configured and ready to use, review the AWS Ground Station CLI Command
[Page 44] Reference page to familiarize yourself with available commands. Follow the AWS CLI command
[Page 44] structure when using this service and prefix your commands with groundstation to specify AWS
[Page 44] Ground Station as the service you want to use. For more information on the AWS CLI command
[Page 44] structure, see Command Structure in the AWS CLI page. An example command structure is
[Page 44] aws groundstation <command> <subcommand> [options and parameters]
[Page 44] First you need to get the ARN for the satellite(s) you wish to tag. This can be done via the list-
[Page 44] satellites API in the AWS CLI:
[Page 44] aws groundstation list-satellites --region us-west-2
[Page 44] Running the above CLI command will return an output similar to this:
[Page 44] (Optional) Naming satellites 36

[Page 45] "satelliteId": "11111111-2222-3333-4444-555555555555"
[Page 45] Find the satellite you wish to tag and note down the satelliteArn. One important caveat
[Page 45] for tagging is that the tag-resource API requires a regional ARN, and the ARN returned by list-
[Page 45] satellites is global. For the next step, you should augment the ARN with the region you would like
[Page 45] to see the tag in (likely the region you schedule in). For this example, we are using us-west-2.
[Page 45] With this change, the ARN will go from:
[Page 45] arn:aws:groundstation::111111111111:satellite/11111111-2222-3333-4444-555555555555

[Page 45] west-2:111111111111:satellite/11111111-2222-3333-4444-555555555555

[Page 45] the key. Additionally, because we are using the AWS CLI, the quotation marks must be escaped with
[Page 45] a backslash. The tag will look something like:

[Page 46] Change the Name For a Satellite
[Page 46] If you want to change the name for a satellite, you can simply call tag-resource with the satellite
[Page 46] ARN again with the same “Name” key, but with a different value in the tag. This will update the
[Page 46] existing tag and show the new name in the console. An example call for this looks like:
[Page 46] aws groundstation tag-resource --region us-west-2 --resource-arn
[Page 46] arn:aws:groundstation:us-
[Page 46] west-2:111111111111:satellite/11111111-2222-3333-4444-555555555555 --tags
[Page 46] Remove the Name For a Satellite
[Page 46] The name set for a satellite can be removed with the untag-resource API. This API needs the
[Page 46] satellite ARN with the region the tag is in, and a list of tag keys. For the name, the tag key is
[Page 46] “Name”. An example call to this API using the AWS CLI looks like:
[Page 46] aws groundstation untag-resource --region us-west-2 --resource-arn
[Page 46] arn:aws:groundstation:us-
[Page 46] west-2:111111111111:satellite/11111111-2222-3333-4444-555555555555 --tag-keys Name

[Page 47] Plan your dataflow communication paths
[Page 47] You have the choice between synchronous and asynchronous communication for each
[Page 47] communication path on your satellite. Depending on your satellite and your use case, you may
[Page 47] require one or both types. Synchronous communication paths allow for near real-time uplink
[Page 47] as well as narrowband and wideband downlink operations. Asynchronous communication paths
[Page 47] support narrowband and wideband downlink operations only.
[Page 47] Asynchronous data delivery
[Page 47] With data delivery to Amazon S3, your contact data is delivered asynchronously to an Amazon
[Page 47] S3 bucket in your account. Your contact data is delivered as packet capture (pcap) files to allow
[Page 47] replaying the contact data into a Software Defined Radio (SDR) or to extract the payload data
[Page 47] from the pcap files for processing. The pcap files are delivered to your Amazon S3 bucket every
[Page 47] 30 seconds as contact data is received by the antenna hardware to allow processing contact
[Page 47] data during the contact if desired. Once received, you can process the data using your own post-
[Page 47] processing software or use other AWS services like Amazon SageMaker AI or Amazon Rekognition.
[Page 47] Data delivery to Amazon S3 is only available for downlinking data from your satellite; it is not
[Page 47] possible to uplink data to your satellite from Amazon S3.
[Page 47] Plan your dataflow communication paths 39
[Page 48] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 48] To utilize this path, you will use need to create an Amazon S3 bucket for AWS Ground Station to
[Page 48] deliver the data into. In the next step, you'll also need to create a S3 Recording Config in the next
[Page 48] step. Please reference the Amazon S3 Recording Config for restrictions on bucket naming and how
[Page 48] to specify the naming convention used for your files.
[Page 48] Synchronous data delivery
[Page 48] With data delivery to Amazon EC2, your contact data is streamed to and from your Amazon EC2
[Page 48] instance. You can process your data in real-time on your Amazon EC2 instance or forward the data
[Page 48] To utilize a synchronous path, you will use need to set up and configure your Amazon EC2 instances
[Page 48] and create one or more Dataflow Endpoint Groups. To configure your Amazon EC2 instance
[Page 48] reference the Set up and configure Amazon EC2. To create your Dataflow Endpoint Group, please
[Page 48] reference the Use AWS Ground Station Dataflow endpoint groups.
[Page 48] The following shows the communication path if you are using the dataflow endpoint configuration.
[Page 48] Synchronous data delivery 40

[Page 49] By this step you have identified the satellite, the communication paths, and the IAM, Amazon EC2,
[Page 49] and Amazon S3 resources as needed. In this step you will create AWS Ground Station configs that
[Page 49] store their respective parameters.
[Page 49] Data delivery configs
[Page 49] The first configs to create relate to where and how you want data delivered. Using the information
[Page 49] from the previous step you will construct many of the following configuration types.
[Page 49] • Amazon S3 Recording Config - Deliver data to your Amazon S3 bucket.
[Page 49] • Dataflow Endpoint Config - Deliver data to your Amazon EC2 instance.
[Page 49] The satellite configs relate how AWS Ground Station can communicate with your satellite. You will
[Page 49] reference the information you gathered in Onboard satellite.
[Page 49] • Tracking Config - Sets preference for how your vehicle is physically tracked during a contact.
[Page 49] This is required for mission profile construction.
[Page 49] • Antenna Downlink Config - Deliver digitized radio frequency data.
[Page 49] • Antenna Downlink Demod Decode Config - Deliver demodulated and decoded radio frequency
[Page 49] • Antenna Uplink Config - Uplink data to your satellite.
[Page 49] • Antenna Uplink Echo Config - Deliver an echo of your uplink signal data.
[Page 50] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 50] Create mission profile
[Page 50] With the configs constructed in the previous step, you have identified how to track your satellite
[Page 50] and the possible ways to communicate with your satellite. In this step you will construct one or
[Page 50] more mission profiles. A mission profile represents the aggregation of the possible configs into an
[Page 50] expected behavior that can be then scheduled and operated on.
[Page 50] For the latest parameters, please reference the AWS::GroundStation::MissionProfile
[Page 50] CloudFormation resource type
[Page 50] 1. Name your mission profile. This allows you to quickly understand its usage within your system.
[Page 50] For example, you may have a satellite-wideband-narrowband-nominal-operations and a
[Page 50] satellite-narrowband-emergency-operations if you have a separate narrowband carrier for
[Page 50] emergency operations.
[Page 50] 2. Set your tracking config.
[Page 50] 3. Set your minimum viable contact durations. This allows you to filter potential contacts to meet
[Page 50] 4. Set your streamsKmsKey and streamsKmsRole that are used to encrypt your data during
[Page 50] transit. This is used for all AWS Ground Station Agent dataflows.
[Page 50] 5. Set your dataflows. Create your dataflows to match your carrier signals using the configs you
[Page 50] created in the previous step.
[Page 50] 6. [Optional] Set your pre-pass and post-pass contact duration seconds. This is used to emit per-
[Page 50] contact events prior-to and after the contact, respectively. See Automate AWS Ground Station
[Page 50] with Events for more information.
[Page 50] 7. [Optional] You can associate Tags to your mission profile. These can be used to help
[Page 50] programmatically differentiate your mission profiles.
[Page 50] You can reference the Example mission profile configurations, to see just some of the potential

[Page 52] AWS Ground Station Locations
[Page 52] AWS Ground Station provides a global network of ground stations in close proximity to our global
[Page 52] network of AWS infrastructure regions. You can configure your use of these locations from any
[Page 52] supported AWS Region. This includes the AWS Region in which data is delivered.
[Page 52] Finding the AWS region for a ground station location
[Page 52] The AWS Ground Station global network includes ground station locations that are not physically
[Page 52] located in the AWS Region to which they are connected. The list of ground stations that you have
[Page 52] access to can be retrieved via the AWS SDK ListGroundStation response. The full list of ground
[Page 52] station locations is presented below, with more coming soon. Please refer to the onboarding guide
[Page 52] to add or modify site approvals for your satellites.

[Page 54] AWS Ground Station supported AWS regions
[Page 54] You can deliver data and configure your Contacts via the AWS SDK or the AWS Ground Station
[Page 54] console from supported AWS Regions. You can view the supported regions and their associated
[Page 54] endpoints at the AWS Ground Station endpoints and quotas.
[Page 54] Digital twin availability
[Page 54] Use the AWS Ground Station digital twin feature is available in all AWS Regions where AWS Ground
[Page 54] Station is available. Digital twin ground stations are exact copies of production ground stations
[Page 54] with a modifying prefix to Ground Station Name of “Digital Twin ”. For example, "Digital Twin Ohio
[Page 54] 1" is a digital twin ground station that is an exact copy of the "Ohio 1" production ground station.
[Page 54] AWS Ground Station site masks
[Page 54] Each AWS Ground Station antenna location has associated site masks. These masks block antennas
[Page 54] at that location from transmitting or receiving when pointing in some directions, typically close to
[Page 54] the horizon. The masks may take into account:
[Page 54] • Features of the geographic terrain surrounding the antenna – For example, this includes
[Page 54] things like mountains or buildings, that would block a radio frequency (RF) signal or prevent
[Page 54] • Radio Frequency Interference (RFI) – This affects both the ability to receive (external RFI sources
[Page 54] impacting a downlink signal into AWS Ground Station antennas) and transmit (the RF signal
[Page 54] transmitted by AWS Ground Station antennas adversely impacting external receivers).
[Page 54] • Legal authorizations – Local site authorizations to operate AWS Ground Station in each region
[Page 54] may include specific restrictions, such as a minimum elevation angle for transmitting.
[Page 54] These site masks may change over time. For example, new buildings could be constructed near an
[Page 54] antenna location, RFI sources may change, or legal authorization may be renewed with different
[Page 54] AWS Ground Station supported AWS Regions 46
[Page 55] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 55] restrictions. The AWS Ground Station site masks are available to you under a non-disclosure
[Page 55] Customer-specific masks
[Page 55] In addition to the AWS Ground Station site masks at each site, you may have additional masks
[Page 55] due to restrictions on your own legal authorization to communicate with your satellites in a given
[Page 55] region. Such masks can be configured in AWS Ground Station on a case-by-case basis to ensure
[Page 55] compliance when using AWS Ground Station to communicate with these satellites. Contact the
[Page 55] AWS Ground Station team for details.
[Page 55] Impact of site masks on available contact times
[Page 55] There are two kinds of site masks: uplink (transmit) site masks, and downlink (receive) site masks.
[Page 55] When listing available contact times using the ListContacts operation, AWS Ground Station will
[Page 55] return visibility times based on when your satellite will rise above and set below the downlink
[Page 55] mask. Available contact times are based on this downlink mask visibility window. This ensures that
[Page 55] you do not reserve time when your satellite is below the downlink mask.
[Page 55] Uplink site masks are not applied to the available contact times, even if the Mission Profile includes
[Page 55] an Antenna Uplink Config in a dataflow edge. This allows you to use all available contact time
[Page 55] for downlink, even if uplink may not be available for portions of that time due to the uplink site
[Page 55] mask. However, the uplink signal may not be transmitted for some or all of the time reserved for a
[Page 55] satellite contact. You are responsible for accounting for the provided uplink mask when scheduling
[Page 55] uplink transmissions.
[Page 55] The portion of a contact that is unavailable for uplink varies depending on the satellite trajectory
[Page 55] during the contact, relative to the uplink site mask at the antenna location. In regions where the
[Page 55] uplink and downlink site masks are similar, this duration will typically be short. In other regions,
[Page 55] where the uplink mask may be considerably higher than the downlink site mask, this could result
[Page 55] in significant portions, or even all, of the contact duration being unavailable for uplink. The full
[Page 55] contact time is billed to you, even if portions of the reserved time are unavailable for uplink.
[Page 55] AWS Ground Station Site Capabilities
[Page 55] To simplify your experience, AWS Ground Station determines a common set of capabilities for
[Page 55] an antenna type and then deploys multiple antenna to a ground station location. Part of the
[Page 55] onboarding steps ensures your satellite is compatible with the antenna types at a specific location.
[Page 55] Customer-specific masks 47

[Page 56] experience at a particular ground station location remains the same over time regardless of which
[Page 56] antennas are being used. The specific performance of your contact will vary due to a wide variety
[Page 56] of environmental concerns such as weather at the site.

[Page 59] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 59] Understand how AWS Ground Station uses ephemerides
[Page 59] An ephemeris, plural ephemerides, is a file or data structure providing the trajectory of
[Page 59] astronomical objects. Historically, this file only referred to tabular data but, gradually, it has come
[Page 59] to direct to a wide variety of data files indicating a spacecraft trajectory.
[Page 59] The Ephemeris API allows custom ephemerides to be uploaded to AWS Ground Station for use with
[Page 59] a satellite. These ephemerides override the default ephemerides from Space-Track (see: Default
[Page 59] ephemeris data). We support receiving ephemeris data in Orbit Ephemeris Message (OEM), two-line
[Page 59] element (TLE), and azimuth elevation formats.
[Page 59] AWS Ground Station uses ephemeris data to determine when contacts become available based on
[Page 59] the provided ephemeris and correctly command antennas in the AWS Ground Station network. By
[Page 59] default, no action is required to provide AWS Ground Station with ephemerides if your satellite has
[Page 59] an assigned NORAD ID.
[Page 59] Uploading custom ephemerides can improve the quality of tracking, handle early operations where
[Page 59] no Space-Track ephemerides are available to AWS Ground Station, and account for maneuvers.
[Page 59] Alternatively, AWS Ground Station supports an azimuth elevation format, which allow you to
[Page 59] directly specify antenna pointing directions without providing satellite orbital information. This
[Page 59] is useful for scenarios where precise antenna pointing is required because satellite trajectory
[Page 59] information is imprecise or unknown.
[Page 59] • Default ephemeris data
[Page 59] • Provide custom ephemeris data
[Page 59] • Reserve contacts with custom ephemeris
[Page 59] • Understand which ephemeris is used
[Page 59] • Get the current ephemeris for a satellite
[Page 59] • Revert to default ephemeris data
[Page 59] Default ephemeris data
[Page 59] By default, AWS Ground Station uses publicly available data from Space-Track, and no action is
[Page 59] required to supply AWS Ground Station with these default ephemerides. These ephemerides are
[Page 59] Default ephemeris data 51
[Page 60] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 60] two-line element sets (TLEs) associated with your satellite's NORAD ID. All default ephemerides
[Page 60] have a priority of 0. As a result, they will be overridden, always, by any non-expired, custom
[Page 60] ephemerides uploaded via the ephemeris API, which must always have a priority of 1, or greater.
[Page 60] Satellites without a NORAD ID must upload custom ephemeris data to AWS Ground Station. For
[Page 60] example, satellites that have just launched or that are intentionally omitted from the Space-Track
[Page 60] catalog would have no NORAD ID and would need to have custom ephemerides uploaded. For
[Page 60] more information on providing custom ephemeris data, see: Providing Custom Ephemeris Data.
[Page 60] Provide custom ephemeris data
[Page 60] The ephemeris API is currently in a Preview state
[Page 60] Access to the Ephemeris API is provided only on an as-needed basis. If you require the ability
[Page 60] to upload custom ephemeris data, please open an AWS Support ticket through the AWS
[Page 60] Support Center Console. Our team will work with you to enable this capability for your specific
[Page 60] The Ephemeris API allows custom ephemerides to be uploaded to AWS Ground Station for use with
[Page 60] a satellite. These ephemerides override the default ephemerides from Space-Track (see: Default
[Page 60] ephemeris data). We support receiving ephemeris data in Orbit Ephemeris Message (OEM), two-line
[Page 60] element (TLE), and azimuth elevation formats.
[Page 60] AWS Ground Station treats ephemerides as Individualized Usage Data. If you use this optional
[Page 60] feature, AWS will use your ephemeris data to provide troubleshooting support.
[Page 60] Uploading custom ephemerides can improve the quality of tracking, handle operations where no
[Page 60] Space-Track ephemerides are available to AWS Ground Station, and account for maneuvers.
[Page 60] To troubleshoot an invalid ephemeris see: Troubleshoot invalid ephemerides
[Page 60] Provide custom ephemeris data 52

[Page 61] Provide TLE ephemeris data
[Page 61] The ephemeris API is currently in a Preview state
[Page 61] Access to the Ephemeris API is provided only on an as-needed basis. If you require the ability
[Page 61] to upload custom ephemeris data, please open an AWS Support ticket through the AWS
[Page 61] Support Center Console. Our team will work with you to enable this capability for your specific
[Page 61] Two-line element (TLE) sets are a standardized format for describing satellite orbits. The Ephemeris
[Page 61] API allows TLE ephemerides to be uploaded to AWS Ground Station for use with a satellite. These
[Page 61] ephemerides override the default ephemerides from Space-Track (see: Default ephemeris data).
[Page 61] AWS Ground Station treats ephemerides as Individualized Usage Data. If you use this optional
[Page 61] feature, AWS will use your ephemeris data to provide troubleshooting support.
[Page 61] Uploading custom TLE ephemerides can improve the quality of tracking, handle early operations
[Page 61] where no Space-Track ephemerides are available to AWS Ground Station, and account for
[Page 61] When providing custom ephemeris before a satellite catalog number is assigned for your
[Page 61] satellite, you can use 00000 for the satellite catalog number field of the TLE, and 000 for
[Page 61] the launch number portion of the international designator field of the TLE (e.g. 24000A for
[Page 61] a vehicle launched in 2024).
[Page 61] Example: Using customer-provided ephemerides with AWS Ground Station 53
[Page 62] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 62] For more information about the format of TLEs, see Two-line element set.
[Page 62] Creating a TLE ephemeris
[Page 62] A TLE ephemeris can be created using the CreateEphemeris action in the AWS Ground Station API.
[Page 62] This action will upload an ephemeris using data either in the request body or from a specified S3
[Page 62] It is important to note that uploading an ephemeris sets the ephemeris to VALIDATING and
[Page 62] starts an asynchronous workflow that will validate and generate potential contacts from your
[Page 62] ephemeris. Only once an ephemeris has passed this workflow and become ENABLED will it be
[Page 62] used for contacts. You should poll DescribeEphemeris for the ephemeris status or use CloudWatch
[Page 62] events to track the ephemeris' status changes.
[Page 62] To troubleshoot an invalid ephemeris see: Troubleshoot invalid ephemerides
[Page 62] Example: Create a two-line element (TLE) set ephemeris via API
[Page 62] The AWS SDKs, and CLI can be used to upload a two line element (TLE) set ephemeris to AWS
[Page 62] Ground Station via the CreateEphemeris call. This ephemeris will be used in place of the default
[Page 62] ephemeris data for a satellite (see Default ephemeris data). This example shows how to do this
[Page 62] using the AWS SDK for Python (Boto3).
[Page 62] A TLE set is a JSON formatted object that strings one or more TLEs together to construct a
[Page 62] continuous trajectory. The TLEs in the TLE set must form a continuous set that we can use to
[Page 62] construct a trajectory (i.e. no gaps in time between TLEs in a TLE set). An example TLE set is shown
[Page 62] "tleLine1": "1 25994U 99068A 20318.54719794 .00000075 00000-0 26688-4 0
[Page 62] "tleLine2": "2 25994 98.2007 30.6589 0001234 89.2782 18.9934
[Page 62] Provide TLE ephemeris data 54
[Page 63] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 63] "tleLine1": "1 25994U 99068A 20318.54719794 .00000075 00000-0 26688-4 0
[Page 63] "tleLine2": "2 25994 98.2007 30.6589 0001234 89.2782 18.9934
[Page 63] The time ranges of the TLEs in a TLE set must match up exactly to be a valid, continuous

[Page 68] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 68] Orbit Ephemeris Message (OEM) is a standardized format for representing spacecraft trajectory
[Page 68] data. The Ephemeris API allows OEM ephemerides to be uploaded to AWS Ground Station for
[Page 68] use with a satellite. These ephemerides override the default ephemerides from Space-Track (see:
[Page 68] Default ephemeris data).
[Page 68] AWS Ground Station treats ephemerides as Individualized Usage Data. If you use this optional
[Page 68] feature, AWS will use your ephemeris data to provide troubleshooting support.
[Page 68] Uploading custom OEM ephemerides can improve the quality of tracking, handle early operations
[Page 68] where no Space-Track ephemerides are available to AWS Ground Station, and account for
[Page 68] When providing custom ephemeris before a satellite catalog number is assigned for your
[Page 68] satellite, you can use satelliteId for the OBJECT_ID portion of the OEM.
[Page 68] For more information about the format of OEMs, see OEM ephemeris format.
[Page 68] AWS Ground Station processes OEM Customer Provided Ephemerides according to the CCSDS
[Page 68] standard with some extra restrictions. OEM files should be in KVN format. The following table
[Page 68] outlines the different fields in an OEM and how AWS Ground Station differs from the CCSDS
[Page 68] Section Field CCSDS required AWS Ground Notes
[Page 68] Header CCSDS_OEM Yes Yes Required value:
[Page 68] CREATION_DATE Yes Yes
[Page 68] Provide OEM ephemeris data 60
[Page 69] AWS Ground Station User Guide

[Page 71] * If any rows that aren't supported by AWS Ground Station are included in the provided OEM, the
[Page 71] OEM will fail validation.
[Page 71] The important deviations from the CCSDS standard for AWS Ground Station are:
[Page 71] • CCSDS_OEM_VERS is required to be 2.0.
[Page 71] • REF_FRAME is required to be either EME2000 or ITRF2000.
[Page 71] • REF_FRAME_EPOCH is not supported by AWS Ground Station.
[Page 71] • CENTER_NAME is required to be Earth.
[Page 71] • TIME_SYSTEM is required to be UTC.
[Page 71] • INTERPOLATION and INTERPOLATION_DEGREE are both required for AWS Ground Station
[Page 71] customer provided ephemeris.

[Page 76] Provide azimuth elevation ephemeris data
[Page 76] The azimuth elevation ephemeris feature is currently in a Preview state and requires explicit
[Page 76] Azimuth elevation ephemeris functionality is under strict access control for a limited
[Page 76] number of pre-determined, specialized use cases. Access is significantly more restrictive
[Page 76] than for standard customer-provided ephemeris capabilities. For more information about
[Page 76] approved use cases and the access request process, please open an AWS Support ticket
[Page 76] through the AWS Support Center Console. Our team will guide you through the approval
[Page 76] process for specialized use cases.
[Page 76] Azimuth elevation ephemeris provides a way to directly specify antenna pointing directions
[Page 76] without providing satellite orbital information. Instead of uploading ephemeris data that describes
[Page 76] a satellite's orbit, you provide time-tagged azimuth and elevation angles that tell the antenna
[Page 76] exactly where to point throughout a contact.
[Page 76] AWS Ground Station treats ephemerides as Individualized Usage Data. If you use this optional
[Page 76] feature, AWS will use your ephemeris data to provide troubleshooting support.
[Page 76] This approach is particularly useful for the following scenarios:
[Page 76] • Early operations support: During Launch and Early Orbit Phase (LEOP) when precise orbital data is
[Page 76] unavailable, or orbital parameters are rapidly changing.
[Page 76] • Custom pointing patterns: Implementing specific pointing sequences for antenna testing or non-
[Page 76] When using azimuth elevation ephemeris, the satellite ARN may be omitted from the
[Page 76] contact reservation request. If the satellite ARN is not omitted, it will still be included as
[Page 76] part of the contact data, but the azimuth elevation ephemeris will be used for antenna
[Page 76] pointing rather than performing ephemeris priority resolution. The azimuth elevation
[Page 76] Provide azimuth elevation ephemeris data 68
[Page 77] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 77] ephemeris is associated with a specific ground station and defines the antenna pointing
[Page 77] directions for that location.
[Page 77] Azimuth elevation ephemeris data format
[Page 77] Azimuth elevation ephemeris data consists of time-tagged azimuth and elevation values organized
[Page 77] into segments. Each segment contains a series of azimuth and elevation angles that cover a specific
[Page 77] The key components of azimuth elevation ephemeris data are:
[Page 77] • Ground Station: The specific ground station where this azimuth elevation ephemeris will be used.
[Page 77] • Angle Unit: The unit of measurement for angles (DEGREE_ANGLE or RADIAN).
[Page 77] • Segments: One or more time-bounded collections of azimuth and elevation angles.
[Page 77] • Time-tagged angles: Individual azimuth and elevation values with associated timestamps.
[Page 77] Each segment requires:
[Page 77] • A reference epoch (the base time for the segment)
[Page 77] • A valid time range (start and end times for the segment)
[Page 77] • At least 5 time-tagged azimuth/elevation pairs
[Page 77] Azimuth elevation constraints:
[Page 77] • Azimuth in degrees: -180° to 360°
[Page 77] • Azimuth in radians: -π to 2π
[Page 77] • Elevation in degrees: -90° to 90°
[Page 77] • Elevation in radians: -π/2 to π/2
[Page 77] • Time values must be in ascending order within each segment
[Page 77] • Segments must not overlap in time
[Page 77] For more information, see the CreateEphemeris API documentation and the TimeAzEl data type.
[Page 77] Provide azimuth elevation ephemeris data 69
[Page 78] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 78] Creating azimuth elevation ephemeris
[Page 78] Azimuth elevation ephemeris is created using the same CreateEphemeris API action, but with the
[Page 78] azEl ephemeris type. The key differences from TLE and OEM ephemeris are:
[Page 78] • You must specify a groundStation parameter
[Page 78] • The satelliteId parameter must be omitted from the request
[Page 78] • Priority settings do not apply (each azimuth elevation ephemeris is specific to a ground station)
[Page 78] • Each segment must contain at least 5 azimuth/elevation points to support 4th order Lagrange
[Page 78] • Additional limits and requirements are detailed in the CreateEphemeris API documentation
[Page 78] It is important to note that uploading an ephemeris sets the ephemeris to VALIDATING and
[Page 78] starts an asynchronous workflow that will validate and generate potential contacts from your
[Page 78] ephemeris. An ephemeris will only be used for contacts after it has passed this workflow and its
[Page 78] status becomes ENABLED. You should poll DescribeEphemeris for the ephemeris status or use
[Page 78] CloudWatch events to track the ephemeris' status changes.
[Page 78] To troubleshoot an invalid ephemeris see: Troubleshoot invalid ephemerides
[Page 78] Example: Create azimuth elevation ephemeris via API
