[Page 86] The ephemeris parameter with the azimuth elevation ID must be provided when listing
[Page 86] contacts to specify which azimuth elevation ephemeris should be used for determining
[Page 86] contact windows. If the satelliteArn is included, it will be associated with the contact
[Page 86] data, but the azimuth elevation ephemeris will be used for antenna pointing rather than
[Page 86] performing ephemeris priority resolution.
[Page 86] Reserve contacts with custom ephemeris
[Page 86] When using custom ephemeris (TLE, OEM, or azimuth elevation), you can reserve contacts using
[Page 86] the ReserveContact API. This section describes two common workflows for reserving contacts and
[Page 86] important considerations for ensuring successful contact scheduling.
[Page 86] AWS Ground Station antennas are shared resources among multiple customers. This means that
[Page 86] even if a contact window appears available when you list contacts, another customer might reserve
[Page 86] it before you do. Therefore, it's crucial to verify that your contact reaches the SCHEDULED state
[Page 86] after reservation and to implement proper monitoring for contact state changes.
[Page 86] Reserve contacts with custom ephemeris 78
[Page 87] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 87] For azimuth elevation ephemeris, the satelliteArn parameter may be omitted from
[Page 87] the ReserveContact request, and you must provide trackingOverrides with the
[Page 87] ephemeris ID. For TLE and OEM ephemeris, you still need to provide the satelliteArn.
[Page 87] Contact reservation workflows
[Page 87] There are two primary workflows for reserving contacts with custom ephemeris:
[Page 87] 1. List-then-reserve workflow: First list available contact windows using ListContacts, then select
[Page 87] and reserve a specific window. This approach is useful when you want to see all available
[Page 87] opportunities before making a selection.
[Page 87] 2. Direct reservation workflow: Directly reserve a contact for a specific time window without first
[Page 87] listing available contacts. This approach is useful when you already know your desired contact
[Page 87] time or are working with predetermined schedules.
[Page 87] Both workflows are valid and the choice depends on your operational requirements. The following
[Page 87] sections provide examples of each approach.
[Page 87] Workflow 1: List available contacts then reserve
[Page 87] This workflow first queries for available contact windows, then reserves a specific window. This is
[Page 87] useful when you want to see all available opportunities before making a selection.
[Page 87] Example: List and reserve with azimuth elevation ephemeris
[Page 87] from datetime import datetime, timezone

[Page 87] name="AzEl Ephemeris for Contact",

[Page 95] Monitoring contact state changes
[Page 95] After reserving a contact, it's important to monitor its state to ensure it successfully transitions
[Page 95] to SCHEDULED and to be notified of any issues. AWS Ground Station emits events to Amazon
[Page 95] EventBridge for all contact state changes.
[Page 95] Contact states follow this lifecycle:
[Page 95] • SCHEDULING - The contact is being processed for scheduling
[Page 95] • SCHEDULED - The contact was successfully scheduled and will execute
[Page 95] • FAILED_TO_SCHEDULE - The contact could not be scheduled (terminal state)
[Page 95] For more information on contact states and lifecycle, see Understand contact lifecycle.
[Page 95] Implementing contact state monitoring with EventBridge
[Page 95] To monitor contact state changes in real-time, you can set up an Amazon EventBridge rule that
[Page 95] triggers a Lambda function whenever a Ground Station contact changes state. This approach is
[Page 95] more efficient and scalable than polling the contact status.
[Page 95] Monitoring contact state changes 87
[Page 96] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 96] 1. Create a Lambda function to process contact state change events
[Page 96] 2. Create an EventBridge rule that matches Ground Station contact state change events
[Page 96] 3. Add the Lambda function as a target for the rule
[Page 96] Example Lambda function handler
[Page 96] For a complete example of a Lambda function that processes contact state change
[Page 96] events, see the GroundStationCloudWatchEventHandlerLambda resource in the
[Page 96] AquaSnppJpssTerraDigIF.yml CloudFormation template. This template is available in the
[Page 96] AWS Ground Station customer onboarding Amazon S3 bucket. For instructions on accessing this
[Page 96] template, see the Putting it together section of the dataflow endpoint example.
[Page 96] EventBridge rule configuration
[Page 96] The EventBridge rule should use the following event pattern to match all Ground Station contact
[Page 96] "source": ["aws.groundstation"],
[Page 96] "detail-type": ["Ground Station Contact State Change"]
[Page 96] To filter for specific states only (e.g., failures), you can add a detail filter:
[Page 96] "source": ["aws.groundstation"],
[Page 96] "detail-type": ["Ground Station Contact State Change"],
[Page 96] "FAILED_TO_SCHEDULE",
[Page 96] Monitoring contact state changes 88
[Page 97] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 97] For detailed instructions on creating EventBridge rules with Lambda targets, see Creating rules that
[Page 97] react to events in the Amazon EventBridge User Guide.
[Page 97] Setting up EventBridge rules for automation
[Page 97] You can create EventBridge rules to automatically respond to contact state changes. For example:
[Page 97] • Send notifications when a contact fails to schedule
[Page 97] • Trigger Lambda functions to prepare resources when a contact enters PREPASS
[Page 97] • Log contact completions for auditing purposes
[Page 97] For detailed information on setting up EventBridge rules for AWS Ground Station events, see
[Page 97] Automate AWS Ground Station with Events.
[Page 97] Best practices and considerations
[Page 97] Handling scheduling conflicts
[Page 97] Since AWS Ground Station antennas are shared resources, a contact window that appears available
[Page 97] in ListContacts might be reserved by another customer before you can reserve it. To handle
[Page 97] 1. Always check the contact status after reservation
[Page 97] 2. Implement retry logic with alternative time windows
[Page 97] 3. Consider reserving contacts well in advance when possible
[Page 97] 4. Use EventBridge events to monitor for FAILED_TO_SCHEDULE states
[Page 97] Ephemeris validation timing
[Page 97] Remember that ephemeris must be in ENABLED state before you can use it to reserve contacts. The
[Page 97] validation process typically takes a few seconds to a few minutes depending on the ephemeris type
[Page 97] and size. Always verify the ephemeris status before attempting to reserve contacts.
[Page 97] Contact timing considerations
[Page 97] When using custom ephemeris:
[Page 97] Best practices and considerations 89
[Page 98] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 98] • Ensure your ephemeris covers the entire contact duration
[Page 98] • For azimuth elevation ephemeris, verify that the angles keep the antenna above the site mask
[Page 98] throughout the contact
[Page 98] • Consider ephemeris expiration times when scheduling future contacts
[Page 98] API differences by ephemeris type
[Page 98] The ReserveContact API behaves differently depending on the ephemeris type:
[Page 98] Ephemeris Type satelliteArn Required trackingOverrides Required
[Page 98] TLE Yes No (optional)
[Page 98] OEM Yes No (optional)
[Page 98] Azimuth Elevation No (optional) Yes
[Page 98] Understand which ephemeris is used
[Page 98] Ephemerides have a priority, expiration time, and enabled flag. Together, these determine which
[Page 98] ephemeris is used for tracking during a contact.
[Page 98] TLE and OEM ephemerides
[Page 98] For OEM and TLE ephemerides, only one ephemeris can be active for each satellite. The ephemeris
[Page 98] that will be used is the highest-priority enabled ephemeris whose expiration time is in the future. A
[Page 98] larger priority value indicates a higher priority. The available contact times returned by ListContacts
[Page 98] are based on this ephemeris. If multiple ENABLED ephemerides have the same priority, the most
[Page 98] recently created or updated ephemeris will be used.
[Page 98] AWS Ground Station has a service quota on the number of ENABLED customer-provided
[Page 98] ephemerides per satellite (see: Service Quotas). To upload ephemeris data after reaching
[Page 98] this quota, delete (using DeleteEphemeris) or disable (using UpdateEphemeris) the lowest-
[Page 98] priority/earliest created customer-provided ephemerides.
[Page 98] Understand which ephemeris is used 90
[Page 99] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 99] If no ephemeris has been created, or if no ephemerides have ENABLED status, AWS Ground
[Page 99] Station will use a default ephemeris for the satellite (from Space-Track), if available. This default
[Page 99] ephemeris has priority 0.
[Page 99] Azimuth elevation ephemerides
[Page 99] Azimuth elevation ephemerides work differently from OEM and TLE ephemerides. Each azimuth
[Page 99] elevation ephemeris is associated with a specific ground station and does not have a priority. When
[Page 99] you reserve a contact with azimuth elevation ephemeris, you explicitly specify which azimuth
[Page 99] elevation ephemeris to use through the trackingOverrides parameter.
[Page 99] Key differences for azimuth elevation ephemerides:
[Page 99] • No priority system - you explicitly select the ephemeris for each contact
[Page 99] • Ground station specific - each ephemeris is associated with a particular ground station
[Page 99] • No automatic fallback - if the specified ephemeris is unavailable, the contact will fail
[Page 99] Azimuth elevation ephemerides do not compete with OEM and TLE ephemerides. They are
[Page 99] selected explicitly when reserving a contact and are only used when tracking overrides are
[Page 99] Effect of new ephemerides on previously scheduled contacts
[Page 99] Use the DescribeContact API to view the effects of new ephemerides on previously scheduled
[Page 99] contacts by returning the active visibility times.
[Page 99] For OEM and TLE ephemerides, contacts scheduled prior to uploading a new ephemeris will retain
[Page 99] the originally scheduled contact time, while the antenna tracking will use the active ephemeris. If
[Page 99] the spacecraft's position, based on the active ephemeris, differs greatly from the prior ephemeris,
[Page 99] this may result in reduced satellite contact time with the antenna due to the spacecraft operating
[Page 99] outside the transmit/receive site mask. Therefore, we recommend that you cancel and reschedule
[Page 99] your future contacts after you upload a new ephemeris that differs greatly from the previous
[Page 99] Azimuth elevation ephemerides 91
[Page 100] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 100] With the DescribeContact API, you can determine the portion of your future contact that is
[Page 100] unusable due to the spacecraft operating outside the transmit/receive site mask by comparing
[Page 100] your scheduled contact startTime and endTime with the returned visibilityStartTime and
[Page 100] visibilityEndTime. If you choose to cancel and reschedule your future contact(s), the contact
[Page 100] time range must not be outside the visibility time range by more than 30 seconds. Cancelled
[Page 100] contacts may incur costs when cancelled too close to the time of contact. For more information on
[Page 100] cancelled contacts see: Ground Station FAQs.
[Page 100] For azimuth elevation ephemerides, scheduled contacts will use the specific ephemeris that was
[Page 100] selected when the contact was reserved. If you need to update the azimuth elevation data for a
[Page 100] scheduled contact, you can cancel and reschedule the contact with a new ephemeris.
[Page 100] Get the current ephemeris for a satellite
[Page 100] The current ephemeris in use by AWS Ground Station for a specific satellite can be retrieved by
[Page 100] calling the GetSatellite or ListSatellites actions. Both of these methods will return metadata for the
[Page 100] ephemeris currently in use. This ephemeris metadata is different for custom ephemerides uploaded
[Page 100] to AWS Ground Station and for default ephemerides.
[Page 100] Azimuth elevation ephemerides are not associated with satellites and therefore are not
[Page 100] returned by GetSatellite or ListSatellites. To retrieve information about azimuth elevation
[Page 100] ephemerides, use the DescribeEphemeris API with the specific ephemeris ID, or use
[Page 100] ListEphemerides to see all available ephemerides for your account.
[Page 100] Default Ephemerides will only include source and epoch fields. The epoch is the epoch of
[Page 100] the two-line element set that was pulled from Space-Track, and it is currently being used for
[Page 100] computing the trajectory of the satellite.
[Page 100] A custom ephemeris will have a source value of CUSTOMER_PROVIDED and will include a unique
[Page 100] identifier in the ephemerisId field. This unique identifier can be used to query for the ephemeris
[Page 100] via the DescribeEphemeris action. An optional name field will be returned if the ephemeris was
[Page 100] assigned a name during upload to AWS Ground Station via the CreateEphemeris action.
[Page 100] It is important to note that ephemerides are updated dynamically by AWS Ground Station so the
[Page 100] returned data is only a snapshot of the ephemeris being used at the time of the call to the API.
[Page 100] Get the current ephemeris for a satellite 92
[Page 101] AWS Ground Station User Guide

[Page 101] "satelliteArn": "arn:aws:groundstation::111122223333:satellite/e1cfe0c7-67f9-4d98-
[Page 101] "noradSatelliteID": 25994,
[Page 101] "currentEphemeris": {

[Page 101] Listing azimuth elevation ephemerides
[Page 101] Since azimuth elevation ephemerides are not associated with satellites, you need to use different
[Page 101] APIs to discover and retrieve information about them:
[Page 101] Example GetSatellite return for a satellite using a default ephemeris 93
[Page 102] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 102] 1. Use ListEphemerides to list all ephemerides in your account, including azimuth elevation
[Page 102] ephemerides. You can filter by status and ephemeris type.
[Page 102] 2. Use DescribeEphemeris with a specific ephemeris ID to get detailed information about an
[Page 102] azimuth elevation ephemeris.
[Page 102] 3. Use DescribeContact with a specific contact ID to get detailed information about an ephemeris
[Page 102] used for the contact.
[Page 102] Example ListEphemerides response including an azimuth elevation ephemeris:
[Page 102] "ephemerisId": "abc12345-6789-def0-1234-5678EXAMPLE",
[Page 102] "ephemerisType": "AZ_EL",
[Page 102] "name": "Azimuth Elevation for Ohio Ground Station",
[Page 102] "creationTime": 1620254718.765
[Page 102] "ephemerisId": "def45678-9012-abc3-4567-8901EXAMPLE",
[Page 102] "ephemerisType": "TLE",
[Page 102] "name": "TLE for Satellite 12345",
[Page 102] "creationTime": 1620254700.123
[Page 102] In the ListEphemerides response, azimuth elevation ephemerides will have a
[Page 102] groundStation field instead of a satelliteId field, making them easy to identify.
[Page 102] Revert to default ephemeris data
[Page 102] When you upload custom ephemeris data it will override the default ephemerides AWS Ground
[Page 102] Station uses for that particular satellite. AWS Ground Station does not use the default ephemeris
[Page 102] again until there are no currently enabled, unexpired customer-provided ephemerides available
[Page 102] Revert to default ephemeris data 94
[Page 103] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 103] for use. AWS Ground Station also does not list contacts past the expiration time of the current
[Page 103] customer-provided ephemeris, even if there is a default ephemeris available past that expiration
[Page 103] Azimuth elevation ephemerides do not have default values and do not override
[Page 103] satellite ephemerides. They are explicitly selected when reserving a contact using
[Page 103] the trackingOverrides parameter. If you no longer wish to use azimuth elevation
[Page 103] ephemeris, simply reserve contacts without specifying tracking overrides, and the system
[Page 103] will use the active satellite ephemeris instead.
[Page 103] Reverting TLE and OEM ephemerides
[Page 103] To revert back to the default Space-Track ephemerides for a satellite, you will need to do one of
[Page 103] • Delete (using DeleteEphemeris) or disable (using UpdateEphemeris) all enabled customer-
[Page 103] provided ephemerides. You can list the customer-provided ephemerides for a satellite using
[Page 103] • Wait for all existing customer-provided ephemerides to expire.
[Page 103] You can confirm that the default ephemeris is being used by calling GetSatellite and verifying that
[Page 103] the source of the current ephemeris for the satellite is SPACE_TRACK. See Default ephemeris data
[Page 103] for more information on default ephemerides.
[Page 103] Managing azimuth elevation ephemerides
[Page 103] Since azimuth elevation ephemerides are explicitly selected for each contact and are not associated
[Page 103] with satellites, there is no concept of "reverting" to a default. Instead, you can manage azimuth
[Page 103] elevation ephemerides as follows:
[Page 103] • To stop using azimuth elevation ephemeris: Simply reserve new contacts without specifying
[Page 103] trackingOverrides and specifying a satelliteArn. The contact will use the active
[Page 103] ephemeris for the specified satellite instead.
[Page 103] Reverting TLE and OEM ephemerides 95
[Page 104] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 104] • To remove unused azimuth elevation ephemerides: Use DeleteEphemeris to delete azimuth
[Page 104] elevation ephemerides that are no longer needed. Note that you cannot delete an ephemeris
[Page 104] that is currently being used by a scheduled contact.
[Page 104] To list all azimuth elevation ephemerides in your account, use ListEphemerides. Azimuth
[Page 104] elevation ephemerides can be identified by the ephemerisType field, or by the presence of a
[Page 104] groundStation field instead of a satelliteId field in the response.
[Page 104] Managing azimuth elevation ephemerides 96
[Page 105] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 105] AWS Ground Station uses a node and edge relationship to construct dataflows to enable stream
[Page 105] processing of your data. Each node is represented by a config which describes its expected
[Page 105] processing. To illustrate this concept, consider a dataflow of antenna-downlink to a s3-
[Page 105] recording. The antenna-downlink node represents the analog to digital transformation of
[Page 105] the radio frequency spectrum per the defined parameters on the config. The s3-recording
[Page 105] represents a compute node which will receive incoming data and store it in your S3 bucket. The
[Page 105] resulting dataflow is an asynchronous data delivery of digitized RF data to an S3 bucket based on
[Page 105] Within your mission profile, you can create many dataflows to meet your needs. The following
[Page 105] sections describe how to set up your other AWS resources to be used with AWS Ground Station and
[Page 105] offers recommendations for constructing dataflows. For detailed information on how each node
[Page 105] behaves, including if it is considered a source or destination node, please see Use AWS Ground
[Page 105] • AWS Ground Station data plane interfaces
[Page 105] • Use cross-region data delivery
[Page 105] • Set up and configure Amazon S3
[Page 105] • Set up and configure Amazon VPC
[Page 105] • Set up and configure Amazon EC2
[Page 105] AWS Ground Station data plane interfaces
[Page 105] The resulting data structure of your chosen dataflow depends on the source of the dataflow.
[Page 105] Details of these formats are provided to you during the onboarding of your satellites. The following
[Page 105] summarizes the formats used for each type of dataflow.
[Page 105] • (Bandwidth less-than-or-equal-to 40MHz) data is delivered as VITA-49 Signal Data/IP Format
[Page 105] • (Bandwidth greater-than 40MHz) data is delivered as AWS Ground Station Class 2 packets.
[Page 105] • antenna-downlink-demod-decode
[Page 105] AWS Ground Station data plane interfaces 97
[Page 106] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 106] • Data is delivered as Demodulated/Decoded Data/IP Format packets.
[Page 106] • Data must be delivered as VITA-49 Signal Data/IP Format packets.
[Page 106] • antenna-uplink-echo
[Page 106] • Data is delivered as VITA-49 Signal Data/IP Format packets.
[Page 106] Use cross-region data delivery
[Page 106] The AWS Ground Station cross-region data delivery feature gives you the flexibility to send your
[Page 106] data from an antenna to any AWS Ground Station supported AWS Region. This means you can
[Page 106] maintain your infrastructure in a single AWS Region and schedule contacts on any AWS Ground
[Page 106] Station Locations you are onboarded to.
[Page 106] When receiving your contact data in an Amazon S3 Bucket, AWS Ground Station will manage all
[Page 106] delivery aspects for you.
[Page 106] To use cross-region data delivery to an Amazon EC2 instance (using either the AWS Ground Station
[Page 106] Agent or a dataflow endpoint), the dataflow-endpoint must be created in your current AWS Region
[Page 106] and your dataflow-endpoint-config must specify the same region. AWS Ground Station will manage
[Page 106] delivering the data cross-region for you.
[Page 106] Set up and configure Amazon S3
[Page 106] You can utilize a Amazon S3 bucket to receive your downlink signals using AWS Ground Station. To
[Page 106] create the destination s3-recording-config, you must be able to specify a Amazon S3 bucket and an
[Page 106] IAM role which authorizes AWS Ground Station to write files to the bucket.
[Page 106] See Amazon S3 Recording Config for restrictions on the Amazon S3 bucket, IAM role, or AWS
[Page 106] Ground Station config creation.
[Page 106] Set up and configure Amazon VPC
[Page 106] A full guide to set up a VPC is beyond the scope of this guide. For an in-depth understanding
[Page 106] please refer to the Amazon VPC User Guide.
[Page 106] In this section, it is described how your Amazon EC2 and dataflow endpoint may exist within a
[Page 106] VPC. AWS Ground Station does not support multiple delivery points for a given dataflow - it is
[Page 106] Using cross-region data delivery 98
[Page 107] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 107] expected that each dataflow terminates to a single EC2 receiver. As we expect a single EC2 receiver,
[Page 107] the configuration is not multi-AZ redundant. For full examples which will use your VPC, please see
[Page 107] Example mission profile configurations.
[Page 107] VPC Configuration with AWS Ground Station Agent
[Page 107] Your satellite data is provided to an AWS Ground Station Agent instance that is proximate to the
[Page 107] antenna. The AWS Ground Station Agent will stripe and then encrypt your data using the AWS KMS
[Page 107] VPC Configuration with AWS Ground Station Agent 99
[Page 108] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 108] key you provide. Each stripe is sent to your Amazon EC2 Elastic IP (EIP) from the source antenna
[Page 108] across the AWS Network backbone. The data arrives at your EC2 instance via the Amazon EC2
[Page 108] Elastic Network Interface (ENI) attached. Once on your EC2 instance, the installed AWS Ground
[Page 108] Station Agent will decrypt your data and perform forward error correction (FEC) to recover any
[Page 108] dropped data, then forward it to the IP and port you specified in your setup.
[Page 108] The below list calls out unique setup considerations when setting up your VPC for AWS Ground
[Page 108] Station Agent delivery.
[Page 108] Security Group - It is recommended you set up a security group dedicated to only AWS Ground
[Page 108] Station traffic. This security group should allow UDP ingress traffic on the same port range you
[Page 108] specify in your Dataflow Endpoint Group. AWS Ground Station maintains an AWS-managed prefix
[Page 108] list to restrict your permissions to only AWS Ground Station IP addresses. See AWS Managed Prefix
[Page 108] Lists for details on how to replace the PrefixListId for your deployment regions.
[Page 108] Elastic Network Interface (ENI) - You will need to associate the above security group with this ENI
[Page 108] and place it in your public subnet.
[Page 108] The default quota for number of security groups attached per ENI is 5. This is an adjustable
[Page 108] limit up to 16, see Amazon VPC Quotas.
[Page 108] The following CloudFormation template demonstrates how to create the infrastructure described

[Page 112] Set up and configure Amazon EC2
[Page 112] Properly configuring your Amazon EC2 instance is required for synchronous delivery of VITA-49
[Page 112] Signal/IP data or VITA-49 Extension data/IP to be delivered via the AWS Ground Station Agent
[Page 112] or a dataflow endpoint. Depending on your specific needs, you may perform the Front End (FE)
[Page 112] processor or Software Defined Radio (SDR) directly on the same instance, or you may need to
[Page 112] utilize additional EC2 instances. Selection and installation of your FE or SDR is beyond the scope of
[Page 112] this user guide. For more information on the specific data formats, see AWS Ground Station data
[Page 112] For information about our service terms, please see AWS Service Terms.
[Page 112] Supplied Common Software
[Page 112] AWS Ground Station provides common software to ease setup of your Amazon EC2 instance.
[Page 112] Set up and configure Amazon EC2 104
[Page 113] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 113] AWS Ground Station Agent
[Page 113] The AWS Ground Station Agent receives Digital Intermediate Frequency (DigIF) downlink data and
[Page 113] egresses decrypted data that enables the following:
[Page 113] • DigIF downlink capability from 40 MHz to 400 MHz of bandwidth.
[Page 113] • High rate, low jitter DigIF data delivery to any public IP (AWS Elastic IP) on the AWS network.
[Page 113] • Reliable data delivery using Forward Error Correction (FEC).
[Page 113] • Secure data delivery using a customer managed AWS KMS key for encryption.
[Page 113] For more information, see AWS Ground Station Agent User Guide.
[Page 113] Dataflow endpoint application
[Page 113] A networking application that is used by AWS Ground Station to send and receive data between
[Page 113] the AWS Ground Station antenna locations, and your Amazon EC2 instances. It can be used for the
[Page 113] uplink and downlink of data.
[Page 113] Software Defined Radio (SDR)
[Page 113] A software defined radio (SDR) that can be used to modulate/demodulate the signal used to
[Page 113] communicate with your satellite.
[Page 113] AWS Ground Station Amazon Machine Images (AMIs)
[Page 113] To reduce the build and configuration times of these installs, AWS Ground Station also offers
[Page 113] preconfigured AMIs. The AMIs with a dataflow endpoint networking application and a software
[Page 113] defined radio (SDR) are made available to your account after your onboarding is complete. They
[Page 113] can be found in the Amazon EC2 console by searching for groundstation in private Amazon
[Page 113] Machine Images (AMIs). The AMIs with AWS Ground Station Agent are public and can be found in
[Page 113] the Amazon EC2 console by searching for groundstation in public Amazon Machine Images (AMIs).
[Page 113] AWS Ground Station Amazon Machine Images (AMIs) 105
[Page 114] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 114] You can enter satellite data, identify antenna locations, communicate, and schedule antenna
[Page 114] time for selected satellites by using the AWS Ground Station console, AWS CLI, or the AWS SDK
[Page 114] in the language of your choice. You can review, cancel, and reschedule contact reservations up to
[Page 114] 15 minutes before contact start*. In addition, you can view the details of your reserved minutes
[Page 114] pricing plan if you are using the AWS Ground Station reserved minutes pricing model.
[Page 114] AWS Ground Station supports cross-region data delivery. The dataflow endpoint configs that are
[Page 114] part of the mission profile you select determine to which region(s) the data is delivered. For more
[Page 114] information about using cross-region data delivery, see Use cross-region data delivery.
[Page 114] To schedule contacts, your resources must be configured. If you have not configured your resources,
[Page 114] see Get started. When ReserveContact is called, AWS Ground Station takes a snapshot of the
[Page 114] mission profile and config resources for use throughout the contact's lifecycle. Changes to these
[Page 114] resources using the UpdateMissionProfile and UpdateConfig APIs will not be reflected in contacts
[Page 114] reserved prior to the updates. If you need the resource changes applied to an already scheduled
[Page 114] contact, you must first cancel the contact using CancelContact, and then reschedule it using
[Page 114] * Cancelled contacts may incur costs when cancelled too close to the time of contact. For more
[Page 114] information on cancelled contacts see: Ground Station FAQs.

[Page 114] Understand contact lifecycle
[Page 114] Understanding the contact lifecycle can help you to automate and troubleshoot various problems
[Page 114] while using AWS Ground Station. The following diagram shows the AWS Ground Station contact
[Page 114] lifecycle as well as Event Bridge Events emitted during the lifecycle. It is important to note that the
[Page 114] COMPLETED, FAILED, FAILED_TO_SCHEDULE, CANCELLED, AWS_CANCELLED, and AWS_FAILED are
[Page 114] terminal states. Contacts will not transition out of a terminal state. See the AWS Ground Station
[Page 114] contact statuses for details on what each status indicates and whether it is stoppable or cancellable

[Page 117] Understand contact billing
[Page 117] With AWS Ground Station, you pay only for the antenna time you use. AWS Ground Station meters
[Page 117] contact usage on a per-minute basis. For each contact, the service calculates the contact duration
[Page 117] from start to end time and rounds up to the nearest minute. This metered duration determines
[Page 117] your charges for that contact.
[Page 117] Your rate depends on two main factors:
[Page 117] • Bandwidth – The amount of bandwidth reserved for the contact (narrowband or wideband)
[Page 117] • Ground station location – Rates vary by ground station location
[Page 117] Understand contact billing 109
[Page 118] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 118] Bandwidth definitions
[Page 118] AWS Ground Station categorizes contacts into two bandwidth tiers based on the instantaneous
[Page 118] • Narrowband – Any contact where the instantaneous bandwidth is less than or equal to 40 MHz
[Page 118] • Wideband – Any contact where the instantaneous bandwidth is greater than 40 MHz
[Page 118] AWS Ground Station offers two scheduling modes:
[Page 118] • On-Demand – Pay for antenna access with no long-term commitments
[Page 118] • Reserved – Provides a discounted rate and improved scheduling compared to On-Demand, with
[Page 118] a monthly commitment. Reserved minute pricing is available for customers who commit to
[Page 118] monthly usage for a certain period of time.
[Page 118] For specific pricing information for your account or to learn more about the Reserved scheduling
[Page 118] mode, contact your AWS representative.
[Page 118] Use of the CancelContact API varies based on the contact state when you call it:
[Page 118] • Before contact start - Cancels the contact entirely
[Page 118] • After contact start and before contact end - Stops the contact in progress
[Page 118] When you cancel a contact, billing depends on your scheduling mode and when you cancel. For
[Page 118] more information, reach out to your AWS representative.
[Page 118] When you stop a contact, you are billed for the portion of the contact that executed, and the
[Page 118] remaining time that is not covered by duplicate contacts. A duplicate contact in this context has
[Page 118] • Scheduled on the same ground station as the original stopped contact
[Page 118] • Scheduled by the same AWS account ID as the original stopped contact
[Page 118] Bandwidth definitions 110
[Page 119] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 119] • Reserved after the command was issued to stop the original contact
[Page 119] The following scenarios demonstrate how this metering works in practice.
[Page 119] Scenario 1: Single contact
[Page 119] You schedule a 150-minute contact on Ground Station Anytown 1 to begin at 14:00 and end at
[Page 119] • First contact: 150 minutes (full duration)
[Page 119] You're billed for 150 minutes. This is the baseline scenario where a contact runs to its scheduled
[Page 119] completion without any stops or cancellations.
[Page 119] Scenario 2: Single stopped contact
[Page 119] You schedule a 150-minute contact on Ground Station Anytown 1 to begin at 14:00 and end at
[Page 119] 16:30. At 15:00, you call the CancelContact API to stop your contact.
[Page 119] Scenario 1: Single contact 111
[Page 120] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 120] • First contact: 150 minutes (full original duration)
[Page 120] You're billed for the full 150 minutes because you stopped the contact but didn't schedule any
[Page 120] duplicate contacts to cover the remaining time (15:00-16:30). When you stop a contact without
[Page 120] scheduling duplicates, you remain responsible for the entire originally scheduled duration.
[Page 120] Scenario 3: Single duplicate
[Page 120] You schedule a 150-minute contact on Ground Station Anytown 1 to begin at 14:00 and end
[Page 120] at 16:30. At 15:00, you call the CancelContact API to stop your first contact. After calling
[Page 120] CancelContact, you schedule another contact on the same Ground Station starting at 15:10 for 150
[Page 120] Scenario 3: Single duplicate 112
[Page 121] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 121] • First contact: 70 minutes (60 minutes executed + 10 minutes of downtime before the second
[Page 121] • Second contact: 150 minutes (full duration)
[Page 121] The second contact is a duplicate because you scheduled it after stopping the first contact. The
[Page 121] duplicate covers the remaining time from 15:10 to 16:30, so you are only billed for the time the
[Page 121] first contact actually ran plus the 10-minute gap between stopping and restarting.
[Page 121] Scenario 4: Short duplicate
[Page 121] You schedule a 150-minute contact on Ground Station Anytown 1 to begin at 14:00 and end
[Page 121] at 16:30. At 15:00, you call the CancelContact API to stop your first contact. After calling
[Page 121] CancelContact, you schedule a 30-minute contact on the same Ground Station starting at 15:10.
[Page 121] Scenario 4: Short duplicate 113
[Page 122] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 122] • First contact: 120 minutes (60 minutes executed + 10 minutes of downtime before the second
[Page 122] contact starts + 50 minutes of remaining time that the duplicate didn't cover)
[Page 122] • Second contact: 30 minutes (full duration)
[Page 122] The duplicate contact only covers 30 minutes (15:10-15:40) of the 90 minutes remaining after you
[Page 122] stopped the first contact. You're billed for both the 10-minute gap before the duplicate starts and
[Page 122] the 50 minutes of uncovered time after the duplicate ends (15:40-16:30).
[Page 122] Scenario 5: Multiple duplicates
[Page 122] You schedule a 150-minute contact on Ground Station Anytown 1 to begin at 14:00 and end
[Page 122] at 16:30. At 15:00, you call the CancelContact API to stop your first contact. After calling
[Page 122] CancelContact, you schedule a 30-minute contact on the same Ground Station starting at 15:10.

[Page 128] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 128] Use the AWS Ground Station digital twin feature
[Page 128] The digital twin feature for AWS Ground Station provides you with an environment where you can
[Page 128] test and integrate your satellite mission management and command and control software. The
[Page 128] digital twin feature allows you to test scheduling, verification of configurations, and proper error
[Page 128] handling without using production antenna capacity. Testing your AWS Ground Station integration
[Page 128] with the digital twin feature enables you to have increased confidence in your system's ability to
[Page 128] manage your satellite operations smoothly. It also allows you to test AWS Ground Station APIs
[Page 128] without using production capacity or requiring spectrum licensing.
[Page 128] To get started, follow Onboard satellite, requesting to be onboarded to the digital twin feature.
[Page 128] Once your satellite is onboarded to the digital twin feature, you can schedule contacts against
[Page 128] digital twin ground stations. The list of ground stations that you have access to can be retrieved
[Page 128] via the AWS SDK ListGroundStations response. Digital twin ground stations are exact copies of the
[Page 128] ground stations listed in AWS Ground Station Locations with a modifying prefix to Ground Station
[Page 128] Name of “Digital Twin ”. This includes their antenna capabilities and metadata, including, but not
[Page 128] limited to, site mask and actual GPS coordinates. At this time, the digital twin feature does not
[Page 128] support data delivery as described in Work with dataflows.
[Page 128] Once onboarded, the digital twin feature emits the same Amazon EventBridge events and API
[Page 128] responses as the production service as described in Automate AWS Ground Station with Events.
[Page 128] These events will allow you to fine tune your configurations and dataflow endpoint groups.
[Page 129] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 129] Understand monitoring with AWS Ground Station
[Page 129] Monitoring is an important part of maintaining the reliability, availability, and performance of AWS
[Page 129] Ground Station. AWS provides the following monitoring tools to watch AWS Ground Station, report
[Page 129] when something is wrong, and take automatic actions when appropriate.
[Page 129] • Amazon EventBridge Events delivers a near real-time stream of system events that describe
[Page 129] changes in AWS resources. EventBridge Events enables automated event-driven computing, as
[Page 129] you can write rules that watch for certain events and trigger automated actions in other AWS
[Page 129] services when these events happen. For more information about EventBridge Events, see the
[Page 129] Amazon EventBridge Events User Guide.
[Page 129] • AWS CloudTrail captures API calls and related events made by or on behalf of your AWS account
[Page 129] and delivers the log files to an Amazon S3 bucket that you specify. You can identify which users
[Page 129] and accounts called AWS, the source IP address from which the calls were made, and when the
[Page 129] calls occurred. For more information about AWS CloudTrail, see the AWS CloudTrail User Guide.
[Page 129] • Amazon CloudWatch Metrics captures metrics for your scheduled contacts when using AWS
[Page 129] Ground Station. CloudWatch Metrics enables you to analyze data based on your channel,
[Page 129] polarization, and satellite ID to identify signal strength and errors in your contacts. For more
[Page 129] information, see Using Amazon CloudWatch metrics.
[Page 129] • AWS User Notifications can be used to set up delivery channels to get notified about AWS Ground
[Page 129] Station events. You receive a notification when an event matches a rule that you specify. You can
[Page 129] receive notifications for events through multiple channels, including email, Amazon Q Developer
[Page 129] in chat applications chat notifications, or AWS Console Mobile Application push notifications.
[Page 129] You can also see notifications in the AWS Console Notification center. User Notifications support
[Page 129] aggregation, which can reduce the number of notifications you receive during specific events.
[Page 129] Use the following topics to monitor AWS Ground Station.
[Page 129] • Automate AWS Ground Station with Events
[Page 129] • Log AWS Ground Station API calls with AWS CloudTrail
[Page 129] • View metrics with Amazon CloudWatch
[Page 130] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 130] Automate AWS Ground Station with Events
[Page 130] This document uses the term “event” throughout. CloudWatch Events and EventBridge are
[Page 130] the same underlying service and API. Rules to match incoming events and route them to
[Page 130] targets for processing can be built using either service.
[Page 130] Events enable you to automate your AWS services and respond automatically to system events
[Page 130] such as application availability issues or resource changes. Events from AWS services are delivered
[Page 130] in near real time. You can write simple rules to indicate which events are of interest to you, and
[Page 130] what automated actions to take when an event matches a rule. Some of the actions that can be
[Page 130] automatically triggered include the following:
[Page 130] • Invoking an AWS Lambda function
[Page 130] • Invoking Amazon EC2 Run Command
[Page 130] • Relaying the event to Amazon Kinesis Data Streams
[Page 130] • Activating an AWS Step Functions state machine
[Page 130] • Notifying an Amazon SNS topic or an Amazon SQS queue
[Page 130] Some examples of using events with AWS Ground Station include:
[Page 130] • Invoking a Lambda function to automate the starting and stopping of Amazon EC2 instances
[Page 130] based off the event state.
[Page 130] • Publishing to an Amazon SNS topic whenever a contact changes states. These topics can be set
[Page 130] up to send out email notices at the beginning or end of contacts.
[Page 130] For more information, see the Amazon EventBridge Events User Guide.
[Page 130] AWS Ground Station Event Types
[Page 130] All events generated by AWS Ground Station have "aws.groundstation" as the value for
[Page 130] Automate with Events 122
[Page 131] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 131] AWS Ground Station emits events related to state changes to support your ability to customize
[Page 131] your automation. Currently, AWS Ground Station supports contact state change events, dataflow
[Page 131] endpoint group change events, and ephemeris state change events. The following sections provide
[Page 131] detailed information about each type.
[Page 131] Contact Event Timeline
[Page 131] AWS Ground Station emits events when your contact changes states. For more information on
[Page 131] what those state changes are, and what the states themselves mean, see Understand contact
[Page 131] lifecycle . Any dataflow endpoint groups being used in your contact have an independent set of
[Page 131] events that are also emitted. During that same timeframe, we also emit events for your dataflow
[Page 131] endpoint group. The precise time of the pre-pass and post-pass events are configurable by you as
[Page 131] you set up your mission profile and dataflow endpoint group.
[Page 131] The following diagram shows the statuses and events emitted for a nominal contact and its
[Page 131] associated dataflow endpoint group.
[Page 131] Ground Station Contact State Change
[Page 131] If you want to perform a specific action when an upcoming contact is changing states,
[Page 131] you can set up a rule to automate this action. This is helpful for when you want to receive
[Page 131] notifications about the state changes of your contact. If you would like to change when you
[Page 131] receive these events, you can modify your mission profile's contactPrePassDurationSeconds and
[Page 131] contactPostPassDurationSeconds. The events are sent to the region that the contact was scheduled

[Page 134] Log AWS Ground Station API calls with AWS CloudTrail
[Page 134] AWS Ground Station is integrated with AWS CloudTrail, a service that provides a record of actions
[Page 134] taken by a user, role, or an AWS service in AWS Ground Station. CloudTrail captures all API calls
[Page 134] for AWS Ground Station as events. The calls captured include calls from the AWS Ground Station
[Page 134] console and code calls to the AWS Ground Station API operations. If you create a trail, you can
[Page 134] enable continuous delivery of CloudTrail events to an Amazon S3 bucket, including events for
[Page 134] AWS Ground Station. If you don't configure a trail, you can still view the most recent events in
[Page 134] the CloudTrail console in Event history. Using the information collected by CloudTrail, you can
[Page 134] determine the request that was made to AWS Ground Station, the IP address from which the
[Page 134] request was made, who made the request, when it was made, and additional details.
[Page 134] To learn more about CloudTrail, see the AWS CloudTrail User Guide.
[Page 134] Log API Calls with CloudTrail 126
[Page 135] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 135] AWS Ground Station Information in CloudTrail
[Page 135] CloudTrail is enabled on your AWS account when you create the account. When activity occurs in
[Page 135] AWS Ground Station, that activity is recorded in a CloudTrail event along with other AWS service
[Page 135] events in Event history. You can view, search, and download recent events in your AWS account.
[Page 135] For more information, see Viewing Events with CloudTrail Event History.
[Page 135] For an ongoing record of events in your AWS account, including events for AWS Ground Station,
[Page 135] create a trail. A trail enables CloudTrail to deliver log files to an Amazon S3 bucket. By default,
[Page 135] when you create a trail in the console, the trail applies to all AWS Regions. The trail logs events
[Page 135] from all Regions in the AWS partition and delivers the log files to the Amazon S3 bucket that you
[Page 135] specify. Additionally, you can configure other AWS services to further analyze and act upon the
[Page 135] event data collected in CloudTrail logs. For more information, see the following:
[Page 135] • Overview for Creating a Trail
[Page 135] • CloudTrail Supported Services and Integrations

[Page 135] • Receiving CloudTrail Log Files from Multiple Regions and Receiving CloudTrail Log Files from
[Page 135] All AWS Ground Station actions are logged by CloudTrail and are documented in the AWS
[Page 135] Ground Station API Reference. For example, calls to the ReserveContact, CancelContact and
[Page 135] ListConfigs actions generate entries in the CloudTrail log files.
[Page 135] Every event or log entry contains information about who generated the request. The identity
[Page 135] information helps you determine the following:
[Page 135] • Whether the request was made with root or AWS Identity and Access Management (IAM) user
[Page 135] • Whether the request was made with temporary security credentials for a role or federated user.
[Page 135] • Whether the request was made by another AWS service.
[Page 135] For more information, see the CloudTrail userIdentity Element.
[Page 135] AWS Ground Station Information in CloudTrail 127
[Page 136] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 136] Understanding AWS Ground Station Log File Entries
[Page 136] A trail is a configuration that enables delivery of events as log files to an Amazon S3 bucket that
[Page 136] you specify. CloudTrail log files contain one or more log entries. An event represents a single
[Page 136] request from any source and includes information about the requested action, the date and time of
[Page 136] the action, request parameters, and so on. CloudTrail log files aren't an ordered stack trace of the
[Page 136] public API calls, so they don't appear in any specific order.

[Page 136] Example: ReserveContact

[Page 136] "creationDate": "2019-05-15T21:11:59Z"

[Page 137] View metrics with Amazon CloudWatch
[Page 137] During a contact, AWS Ground Station automatically captures and sends data to CloudWatch for
[Page 137] analysis. Your data can be viewed in the Amazon CloudWatch console. For more information about
[Page 137] accessing and CloudWatch Metrics, see Using Amazon CloudWatch Metrics.
[Page 137] AWS Ground Station emits CloudWatch metrics to the AWS region associated with
[Page 137] the contact's ground station location, not the AWS region from which the contact was
[Page 137] scheduled. To view metrics for a contact, you must access CloudWatch in the ground station
[Page 137] region. For information about which AWS region is associated with each ground station
[Page 137] location, see Finding the AWS region for a ground station location.

[Page 142] When viewing graphed metrics, it is important to note that the aggregation window determines
[Page 142] how your metrics will be displayed. Each metric in a contact can be displayed as data per second
[Page 142] for 3 hours after the data is received. Your data will be aggregated by CloudWatch Metrics as data
[Page 142] per minute after that 3-hour period has elapsed. If you need to view your metrics on a data per
[Page 142] second measurement, it is recommended to view your data within the 3-hour period after the
[Page 142] data is received or persist it outside of CloudWatch Metrics. For more information on CloudWatch
[Page 142] retention, see Amazon CloudWatch concepts - Metric retention.
[Page 142] In addition, any data captured within the first 60 seconds will not contain enough information to
[Page 142] produce meaningful metrics, and will likely not be displayed. In order to view meaningful metrics, it
[Page 142] is recommended to view your data after 60 seconds has passed.
[Page 143] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 143] For more information about graphing AWS Ground Station metrics in CloudWatch, see Graphing
[Page 143] To view metrics using the console
[Page 143] 1. Determine the AWS region associated with your ground station location. AWS Ground Station
[Page 143] emits CloudWatch metrics in the region associated with your contact's ground station location.
[Page 143] For the list of ground station locations and their associated AWS regions, see Finding the AWS
[Page 143] region for a ground station location.
[Page 143] 2. Open the CloudWatch console.
[Page 143] 3. In the navigation pane, choose Metrics.
[Page 143] 4. Select the GroundStation namespace.
[Page 143] 5. Select your desired metric dimensions (for example, Channel, Polarization, SatelliteId).
[Page 144] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 144] 6. The All metrics tab displays all metrics for that dimension in the namespace. You can do the
[Page 144] a. To sort the table, use the column heading.
[Page 144] b. To graph a metric, select the checkbox associated with the metric. To select all metrics,
[Page 144] select the checkbox in the heading row of the table.
[Page 144] c. To filter by resource, choose the resource ID and then choose Add to search.
[Page 144] d. To filter by metric, choose the metric name and then choose Add to search.
[Page 144] To view metrics using AWS CLI
[Page 144] AWS Ground Station emits CloudWatch metrics in the region associated with your contact's ground
[Page 144] station location. For the list of ground station locations their associated AWS regions, Finding the
[Page 144] AWS region for a ground station location. Replace ground-station-region-code with the
[Page 144] AWS region code for your ground station location (for example, us-west-2 for Oregon 1, Hawaii
[Page 144] 1, or Alaska 1). All subsequent AWS CLI commands in this procedure must use the same region.
[Page 144] 1. Ensure that AWS CLI is installed. For information about installing AWS CLI, see Installing the
[Page 144] 2. Identify the AWS region associated with your ground station location.
[Page 144] 3. Use the get-metric-data method of the CloudWatch CLI to generate a file that can be modified
[Page 144] to specify the metrics that you're interested in, and then be used to query for those metrics.
[Page 144] To do this, run the following: aws cloudwatch get-metric-data --region ground-
[Page 144] station-region-code --generate-cli-skeleton. This will generate output similar to:
[Page 144] "MetricDataQueries": [
[Page 145] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 145] "StartTime": "1970-01-01T00:00:00",
[Page 145] "EndTime": "1970-01-01T00:00:00",
[Page 145] "ScanBy": "TimestampDescending",
[Page 145] 4. List the available CloudWatch metrics by running aws cloudwatch list-metrics --
[Page 145] region ground-station-region-code .
[Page 145] If you've recently used AWS Ground Station, the method should return output that contains
[Page 145] "Namespace": "AWS/GroundStation",
[Page 145] "MetricName": "ReceivedPower",
[Page 145] "Name": "Polarization",
[Page 145] "Name": "SatelliteId",
[Page 146] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 146] "Value": "arn:aws:groundstation::111111111111:satellite/aaaaaaaa-
[Page 146] bbbb-cccc-dddd-eeeeeeeeeeee"
[Page 146] If it's been over 2 weeks since you last used AWS Ground Station, then you will need
[Page 146] to manually inspect the table of available metrics to find the metric names and
[Page 146] dimensions in the AWS/GroundStation metric namespace. For more information
[Page 146] about CloudWatch limitations see: View available metrics
[Page 146] 5. Modify the JSON file you created in step 2 to match the required values from step 3, for
[Page 146] example SatelliteId, and Polarization from your metrics. Also be sure to update the
[Page 146] StartTime, and EndTime values to match your contact. For example:
[Page 146] "MetricDataQueries": [
[Page 146] "Id": "receivedPowerExample",
[Page 146] "Namespace": "AWS/GroundStation",
[Page 146] "MetricName": "ReceivedPower",
[Page 146] "Name": "SatelliteId",
[Page 146] "arn:aws:groundstation::111111111111:satellite/aaaaaaaa-bbbb-cccc-dddd-
[Page 146] "Name": "Polarization",
[Page 147] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 147] "Label": "ReceivedPowerExample",
[Page 147] "StartTime": "2024-02-08T00:00:00",
[Page 147] "EndTime": "2024-04-09T00:00:00"
[Page 147] AWS Ground Station publishes metrics every 1 to 60 seconds, depending on the metric.
[Page 147] Metrics will not be returned if the Period field has a value less than the publishing
[Page 147] period for the metric.
[Page 147] 6. Run aws cloudwatch get-metric-data with the configuration file created in the previous
[Page 147] steps. An example is provided below.
[Page 147] aws cloudwatch get-metric-data --region ground-station-region-code --cli-input-json
[Page 147] file://<nameOfConfigurationFileCreatedInStep2>.json

[Page 149] Security in AWS Ground Station
[Page 149] Cloud security at AWS is the highest priority. As an AWS customer, you will benefit from a data
[Page 149] center and network architecture built to meet the requirements of the most security-sensitive
[Page 149] organizations. AWS provides security-specific tools and features to help you meet your security
[Page 149] objectives. These tools and features include network security, configuration management, access
[Page 149] control, and data security.
[Page 149] When using AWS Ground Station, we recommend that you follow industry best practices and
[Page 149] implement end-to-end encryption. AWS provides APIs for you to integrate encryption and data
[Page 149] protection. For more information about AWS security, see the Introduction to AWS Security
[Page 149] Use the following topics to learn how to secure your resources.
[Page 149] • Identity and Access Management for AWS Ground Station
[Page 149] • AWS managed policies for AWS Ground Station
[Page 149] • Use service-linked roles for Ground Station
[Page 149] • Data encryption at rest for AWS Ground Station
[Page 149] • Data encryption during transit for AWS Ground Station
[Page 149] Identity and Access Management for AWS Ground Station
[Page 149] AWS Identity and Access Management (IAM) is an AWS service that helps an administrator securely
[Page 149] control access to AWS resources. IAM administrators control who can be authenticated (signed in)
[Page 149] and authorized (have permissions) to use AWS Ground Station resources. IAM is an AWS service that
[Page 149] you can use with no additional charge.
[Page 149] • Authenticating with identities
[Page 149] • Managing access using policies
[Page 149] • How AWS Ground Station works with IAM
[Page 149] Identity and Access Management 141
[Page 150] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 150] • Identity-based policy examples for AWS Ground Station
[Page 150] • Troubleshooting AWS Ground Station identity and access
[Page 150] How you use AWS Identity and Access Management (IAM) differs based on your role:
[Page 150] • Service user - request permissions from your administrator if you cannot access features (see
[Page 150] Troubleshooting AWS Ground Station identity and access)
[Page 150] • Service administrator - determine user access and submit permission requests (see How AWS
[Page 150] Ground Station works with IAM)
[Page 150] • IAM administrator - write policies to manage access (see Identity-based policy examples for AWS
[Page 150] Authenticating with identities
[Page 150] Authentication is how you sign in to AWS using your identity credentials. You must be
[Page 150] authenticated as the AWS account root user, an IAM user, or by assuming an IAM role.
[Page 150] You can sign in as a federated identity using credentials from an identity source like AWS
[Page 150] IAM Identity Center (IAM Identity Center), single sign-on authentication, or Google/Facebook
[Page 150] credentials. For more information about signing in, see How to sign in to your AWS account in the
[Page 150] AWS Sign-In User Guide.
[Page 150] For programmatic access, AWS provides an SDK and CLI to cryptographically sign requests. For
[Page 150] more information, see AWS Signature Version 4 for API requests in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 150] AWS account root user
[Page 150] When you create an AWS account, you begin with one sign-in identity called the AWS account root
[Page 150] user that has complete access to all AWS services and resources. We strongly recommend that you
[Page 150] don't use the root user for everyday tasks. For tasks that require root user credentials, see Tasks
[Page 150] that require root user credentials in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 150] As a best practice, require human users to use federation with an identity provider to access AWS
[Page 150] services using temporary credentials.
[Page 151] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 151] A federated identity is a user from your enterprise directory, web identity provider, or Directory
[Page 151] Service that accesses AWS services using credentials from an identity source. Federated identities
[Page 151] assume roles that provide temporary credentials.
[Page 151] For centralized access management, we recommend AWS IAM Identity Center. For more
[Page 151] information, see What is IAM Identity Center? in the AWS IAM Identity Center User Guide.
[Page 151] An IAM user is an identity with specific permissions for a single person or application. We
[Page 151] recommend using temporary credentials instead of IAM users with long-term credentials. For more
[Page 151] information, see Require human users to use federation with an identity provider to access AWS
[Page 151] using temporary credentials in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 151] An IAM group specifies a collection of IAM users and makes permissions easier to manage for large
[Page 151] sets of users. For more information, see Use cases for IAM users in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 151] An IAM role is an identity with specific permissions that provides temporary credentials. You can
[Page 151] assume a role by switching from a user to an IAM role (console) or by calling an AWS CLI or AWS
[Page 151] API operation. For more information, see Methods to assume a role in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 151] IAM roles are useful for federated user access, temporary IAM user permissions, cross-account
[Page 151] access, cross-service access, and applications running on Amazon EC2. For more information, see
[Page 151] Cross account resource access in IAM in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 151] Managing access using policies
[Page 151] You control access in AWS by creating policies and attaching them to AWS identities or resources.
[Page 151] A policy defines permissions when associated with an identity or resource. AWS evaluates these
[Page 151] policies when a principal makes a request. Most policies are stored in AWS as JSON documents. For
[Page 151] more information about JSON policy documents, see Overview of JSON policies in the IAM User
[Page 151] Using policies, administrators specify who has access to what by defining which principal can
[Page 151] perform actions on what resources, and under what conditions.
[Page 151] By default, users and roles have no permissions. An IAM administrator creates IAM policies and
[Page 151] adds them to roles, which users can then assume. IAM policies define permissions regardless of the
[Page 151] method used to perform the operation.
[Page 151] Managing access using policies 143
[Page 152] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 152] Identity-based policies
[Page 152] Identity-based policies are JSON permissions policy documents that you attach to an identity (user,
[Page 152] group, or role). These policies control what actions identities can perform, on which resources, and
[Page 152] under what conditions. To learn how to create an identity-based policy, see Define custom IAM
[Page 152] permissions with customer managed policies in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 152] Identity-based policies can be inline policies (embedded directly into a single identity) or managed
[Page 152] policies (standalone policies attached to multiple identities). To learn how to choose between
[Page 152] managed and inline policies, see Choose between managed policies and inline policies in the IAM
[Page 152] Resource-based policies
[Page 152] Resource-based policies are JSON policy documents that you attach to a resource. Examples
[Page 152] include IAM role trust policies and Amazon S3 bucket policies. In services that support resource-
[Page 152] based policies, service administrators can use them to control access to a specific resource. You
[Page 152] must specify a principal in a resource-based policy.
[Page 152] Resource-based policies are inline policies that are located in that service. You can't use AWS
[Page 152] managed policies from IAM in a resource-based policy.
[Page 152] AWS supports additional policy types that can set the maximum permissions granted by more
[Page 152] • Permissions boundaries – Set the maximum permissions that an identity-based policy can grant
[Page 152] to an IAM entity. For more information, see Permissions boundaries for IAM entities in the IAM
[Page 152] • Service control policies (SCPs) – Specify the maximum permissions for an organization or
[Page 152] organizational unit in AWS Organizations. For more information, see Service control policies in
[Page 152] the AWS Organizations User Guide.
[Page 152] • Resource control policies (RCPs) – Set the maximum available permissions for resources in your
[Page 152] accounts. For more information, see Resource control policies (RCPs) in the AWS Organizations
[Page 152] • Session policies – Advanced policies passed as a parameter when creating a temporary session
[Page 152] for a role or federated user. For more information, see Session policies in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 152] Managing access using policies 144
[Page 153] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 153] Multiple policy types
[Page 153] When multiple types of policies apply to a request, the resulting permissions are more complicated
[Page 153] to understand. To learn how AWS determines whether to allow a request when multiple policy
[Page 153] types are involved, see Policy evaluation logic in the IAM User Guide.

[Page 153] How AWS Ground Station works with IAM 145
[Page 154] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 154] Identity-based policies for AWS Ground Station
[Page 154] Supports identity-based policies: Yes
[Page 154] Identity-based policies are JSON permissions policy documents that you can attach to an identity,
[Page 154] such as an IAM user, group of users, or role. These policies control what actions users and roles can
[Page 154] perform, on which resources, and under what conditions. To learn how to create an identity-based
[Page 154] policy, see Define custom IAM permissions with customer managed policies in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 154] With IAM identity-based policies, you can specify allowed or denied actions and resources as well
[Page 154] as the conditions under which actions are allowed or denied. To learn about all of the elements
[Page 154] that you can use in a JSON policy, see IAM JSON policy elements reference in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 154] Identity-based policy examples for AWS Ground Station
[Page 154] To view examples of AWS Ground Station identity-based policies, see Identity-based policy
[Page 154] examples for AWS Ground Station.
[Page 154] Resource-based policies within AWS Ground Station
[Page 154] Supports resource-based policies: No
[Page 154] Resource-based policies are JSON policy documents that you attach to a resource. Examples of
[Page 154] resource-based policies are IAM role trust policies and Amazon S3 bucket policies. In services that
[Page 154] support resource-based policies, service administrators can use them to control access to a specific
[Page 154] resource. For the resource where the policy is attached, the policy defines what actions a specified
[Page 154] principal can perform on that resource and under what conditions. You must specify a principal
[Page 154] in a resource-based policy. Principals can include accounts, users, roles, federated users, or AWS
[Page 154] To enable cross-account access, you can specify an entire account or IAM entities in another
[Page 154] account as the principal in a resource-based policy. For more information, see Cross account
[Page 154] resource access in IAM in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 154] Policy actions for AWS Ground Station
[Page 154] Supports policy actions: Yes
[Page 154] Administrators can use AWS JSON policies to specify who has access to what. That is, which
[Page 154] principal can perform actions on what resources, and under what conditions.
[Page 154] How AWS Ground Station works with IAM 146
[Page 155] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 155] The Action element of a JSON policy describes the actions that you can use to allow or deny
[Page 155] access in a policy. Include actions in a policy to grant permissions to perform the associated
[Page 155] To see a list of AWS Ground Station actions, see Actions defined by AWS Ground Station in the
[Page 155] Service Authorization Reference.
[Page 155] Policy actions in AWS Ground Station use the following prefix before the action:
[Page 155] To specify multiple actions in a single statement, separate them with commas.
[Page 155] "groundstation:action1",
[Page 155] "groundstation:action2"
[Page 155] To view examples of AWS Ground Station identity-based policies, see Identity-based policy
[Page 155] examples for AWS Ground Station.
[Page 155] Policy resources for AWS Ground Station
[Page 155] Supports policy resources: Yes
[Page 155] Administrators can use AWS JSON policies to specify who has access to what. That is, which
[Page 155] principal can perform actions on what resources, and under what conditions.
[Page 155] The Resource JSON policy element specifies the object or objects to which the action applies. As
[Page 155] a best practice, specify a resource using its Amazon Resource Name (ARN). For actions that don't
[Page 155] support resource-level permissions, use a wildcard (*) to indicate that the statement applies to all
[Page 155] To see a list of AWS Ground Station resource types and their ARNs, see Resources defined by AWS
[Page 155] Ground Station in the Service Authorization Reference. To learn with which actions you can specify
[Page 155] the ARN of each resource, see Actions defined by AWS Ground Station.
[Page 155] How AWS Ground Station works with IAM 147
[Page 156] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 156] To view examples of AWS Ground Station identity-based policies, see Identity-based policy
[Page 156] examples for AWS Ground Station.
[Page 156] Policy condition keys for AWS Ground Station
[Page 156] Supports service-specific policy condition keys: Yes
[Page 156] Administrators can use AWS JSON policies to specify who has access to what. That is, which
[Page 156] principal can perform actions on what resources, and under what conditions.
[Page 156] The Condition element specifies when statements execute based on defined criteria. You can
[Page 156] create conditional expressions that use condition operators, such as equals or less than, to match
[Page 156] the condition in the policy with values in the request. To see all AWS global condition keys, see
[Page 156] AWS global condition context keys in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 156] To see a list of AWS Ground Station condition keys, see Condition keys for AWS Ground Station
[Page 156] in the Service Authorization Reference. To learn with which actions and resources you can use a
[Page 156] condition key, see Actions defined by AWS Ground Station.
[Page 156] To view examples of AWS Ground Station identity-based policies, see Identity-based policy
[Page 156] examples for AWS Ground Station.
[Page 156] ACLs in AWS Ground Station
[Page 156] Access control lists (ACLs) control which principals (account members, users, or roles) have
[Page 156] permissions to access a resource. ACLs are similar to resource-based policies, although they do not
[Page 156] use the JSON policy document format.
[Page 156] ABAC with AWS Ground Station
[Page 156] Supports ABAC (tags in policies): Yes
[Page 156] Attribute-based access control (ABAC) is an authorization strategy that defines permissions based
[Page 156] on attributes called tags. You can attach tags to IAM entities and AWS resources, then design ABAC
[Page 156] policies to allow operations when the principal's tag matches the tag on the resource.
[Page 156] To control access based on tags, you provide tag information in the condition element of a policy
[Page 156] using the aws:ResourceTag/key-name, aws:RequestTag/key-name, or aws:TagKeys
[Page 156] How AWS Ground Station works with IAM 148
[Page 157] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 157] If a service supports all three condition keys for every resource type, then the value is Yes for the
[Page 157] service. If a service supports all three condition keys for only some resource types, then the value is
[Page 157] For more information about ABAC, see Define permissions with ABAC authorization in the IAM User
[Page 157] Guide. To view a tutorial with steps for setting up ABAC, see Use attribute-based access control
[Page 157] (ABAC) in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 157] Using temporary credentials with AWS Ground Station
[Page 157] Supports temporary credentials: Yes
[Page 157] Temporary credentials provide short-term access to AWS resources and are automatically created
[Page 157] when you use federation or switch roles. AWS recommends that you dynamically generate
[Page 157] temporary credentials instead of using long-term access keys. For more information, see
[Page 157] Temporary security credentials in IAM and AWS services that work with IAM in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 157] Cross-service principal permissions for AWS Ground Station
[Page 157] Supports forward access sessions (FAS): Yes
[Page 157] Forward access sessions (FAS) use the permissions of the principal calling an AWS service,
[Page 157] combined with the requesting AWS service to make requests to downstream services. For policy
[Page 157] details when making FAS requests, see Forward access sessions.
[Page 157] Service roles for AWS Ground Station
[Page 157] Supports service roles: No
[Page 157] A service role is an IAM role that a service assumes to perform actions on your behalf. An IAM
[Page 157] administrator can create, modify, and delete a service role from within IAM. For more information,
[Page 157] see Create a role to delegate permissions to an AWS service in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 157] Changing the permissions for a service role might break AWS Ground Station functionality.
[Page 157] Edit service roles only when AWS Ground Station provides guidance to do so.
[Page 157] Service-linked roles for AWS Ground Station
[Page 157] Supports service-linked roles: Yes
[Page 157] How AWS Ground Station works with IAM 149
[Page 158] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 158] A service-linked role is a type of service role that is linked to an AWS service. The service can
[Page 158] assume the role to perform an action on your behalf. Service-linked roles appear in your AWS
[Page 158] account and are owned by the service. An IAM administrator can view, but not edit the permissions
[Page 158] for service-linked roles.
[Page 158] For details about creating or managing service-linked roles, see AWS services that work with IAM.
[Page 158] Find a service in the table that includes a Yes in the Service-linked role column. Choose the Yes
[Page 158] link to view the service-linked role documentation for that service.
[Page 158] Identity-based policy examples for AWS Ground Station
[Page 158] By default, users and roles don't have permission to create or modify AWS Ground Station
[Page 158] resources. To grant users permission to perform actions on the resources that they need, an IAM
[Page 158] administrator can create IAM policies.
[Page 158] To learn how to create an IAM identity-based policy by using these example JSON policy
[Page 158] documents, see Create IAM policies (console) in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 158] For details about actions and resource types defined by AWS Ground Station, including the format
[Page 158] of the ARNs for each of the resource types, see Actions, resources, and condition keys for AWS
[Page 158] Ground Station in the Service Authorization Reference.
[Page 158] • Policy best practices
[Page 158] • Using the AWS Ground Station console
[Page 158] • Allow users to view their own permissions
[Page 158] Policy best practices
[Page 158] Identity-based policies determine whether someone can create, access, or delete AWS Ground
[Page 158] Station resources in your account. These actions can incur costs for your AWS account. When you
[Page 158] create or edit identity-based policies, follow these guidelines and recommendations:
[Page 158] • Get started with AWS managed policies and move toward least-privilege permissions – To
[Page 158] get started granting permissions to your users and workloads, use the AWS managed policies
[Page 158] that grant permissions for many common use cases. They are available in your AWS account. We
[Page 158] recommend that you reduce permissions further by defining AWS customer managed policies
[Page 158] Identity-based policy examples 150
[Page 159] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 159] that are specific to your use cases. For more information, see AWS managed policies or AWS
[Page 159] managed policies for job functions in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 159] • Apply least-privilege permissions – When you set permissions with IAM policies, grant only the
[Page 159] permissions required to perform a task. You do this by defining the actions that can be taken on
[Page 159] specific resources under specific conditions, also known as least-privilege permissions. For more
[Page 159] information about using IAM to apply permissions, see Policies and permissions in IAM in the
[Page 159] • Use conditions in IAM policies to further restrict access – You can add a condition to your
[Page 159] policies to limit access to actions and resources. For example, you can write a policy condition to
[Page 159] specify that all requests must be sent using SSL. You can also use conditions to grant access to
[Page 159] service actions if they are used through a specific AWS service, such as CloudFormation. For more
[Page 159] information, see IAM JSON policy elements: Condition in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 159] • Use IAM Access Analyzer to validate your IAM policies to ensure secure and functional
[Page 159] permissions – IAM Access Analyzer validates new and existing policies so that the policies
[Page 159] adhere to the IAM policy language (JSON) and IAM best practices. IAM Access Analyzer provides
[Page 159] more than 100 policy checks and actionable recommendations to help you author secure and
[Page 159] functional policies. For more information, see Validate policies with IAM Access Analyzer in the
[Page 159] • Require multi-factor authentication (MFA) – If you have a scenario that requires IAM users or
[Page 159] a root user in your AWS account, turn on MFA for additional security. To require MFA when API
[Page 159] operations are called, add MFA conditions to your policies. For more information, see Secure API
[Page 159] access with MFA in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 159] For more information about best practices in IAM, see Security best practices in IAM in the IAM User
[Page 159] Using the AWS Ground Station console
[Page 159] To access the AWS Ground Station console, you must have a minimum set of permissions. These
[Page 159] permissions must allow you to list and view details about the AWS Ground Station resources in
[Page 159] your AWS account. If you create an identity-based policy that is more restrictive than the minimum
[Page 159] required permissions, the console won't function as intended for entities (users or roles) with that
[Page 159] You don't need to allow minimum console permissions for users that are making calls only to the
[Page 159] AWS CLI or the AWS API. Instead, allow access to only the actions that match the API operation
[Page 159] that they're trying to perform.
[Page 159] Identity-based policy examples 151
[Page 160] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 160] To ensure that users and roles can still use the AWS Ground Station console, also attach the AWS
[Page 160] Ground Station ConsoleAccess or ReadOnly AWS managed policy to the entities. For more
[Page 160] information, see Adding permissions to a user in the IAM User Guide.
