[Page 161] Troubleshooting AWS Ground Station identity and access
[Page 161] Use the following information to help you diagnose and fix common issues that you might
[Page 161] encounter when working with AWS Ground Station and IAM.
[Page 161] • I am not authorized to perform an action in AWS Ground Station
[Page 161] • I am not authorized to perform iam:PassRole
[Page 161] • I want to allow people outside of my AWS account to access my AWS Ground Station resources
[Page 161] I am not authorized to perform an action in AWS Ground Station
[Page 161] If you receive an error that you're not authorized to perform an action, your policies must be
[Page 161] updated to allow you to perform the action.
[Page 161] The following example error occurs when the mateojackson IAM user tries to use the console
[Page 161] to view details about a fictional my-example-widget resource but doesn't have the fictional
[Page 161] groundstation:GetWidget permissions.
[Page 161] User: arn:aws:iam::123456789012:user/mateojackson is not authorized to perform:
[Page 161] groundstation:GetWidget on resource: my-example-widget
[Page 161] In this case, the policy for the mateojackson user must be updated to allow access to the my-
[Page 161] example-widget resource by using the groundstation:GetWidget action.
[Page 161] If you need help, contact your AWS administrator. Your administrator is the person who provided
[Page 161] you with your sign-in credentials.
[Page 161] I am not authorized to perform iam:PassRole
[Page 161] If you receive an error that you're not authorized to perform the iam:PassRole action, your
[Page 161] policies must be updated to allow you to pass a role to AWS Ground Station.
[Page 161] Some AWS services allow you to pass an existing role to that service instead of creating a new
[Page 161] service role or service-linked role. To do this, you must have permissions to pass the role to the
[Page 162] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 162] The following example error occurs when an IAM user named marymajor tries to use the console
[Page 162] to perform an action in AWS Ground Station. However, the action requires the service to have
[Page 162] permissions that are granted by a service role. Mary does not have permissions to pass the role to
[Page 162] User: arn:aws:iam::123456789012:user/marymajor is not authorized to perform:
[Page 162] In this case, Mary's policies must be updated to allow her to perform the iam:PassRole action.
[Page 162] If you need help, contact your AWS administrator. Your administrator is the person who provided
[Page 162] you with your sign-in credentials.
[Page 162] I want to allow people outside of my AWS account to access my AWS Ground
[Page 162] You can create a role that users in other accounts or people outside of your organization can use to
[Page 162] access your resources. You can specify who is trusted to assume the role. For services that support
[Page 162] resource-based policies or access control lists (ACLs), you can use those policies to grant people
[Page 162] access to your resources.
[Page 162] To learn more, consult the following:
[Page 162] • To learn whether AWS Ground Station supports these features, see How AWS Ground Station
[Page 162] • To learn how to provide access to your resources across AWS accounts that you own, see
[Page 162] Providing access to an IAM user in another AWS account that you own in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 162] • To learn how to provide access to your resources to third-party AWS accounts, see Providing
[Page 162] access to AWS accounts owned by third parties in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 162] • To learn how to provide access through identity federation, see Providing access to externally
[Page 162] authenticated users (identity federation) in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 162] • To learn the difference between using roles and resource-based policies for cross-account access,
[Page 162] see Cross account resource access in IAM in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 162] AWS managed policies for AWS Ground Station
[Page 162] AWS managed policies 154
[Page 163] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 163] An AWS managed policy is a standalone policy that is created and administered by AWS. AWS
[Page 163] managed policies are designed to provide permissions for many common use cases so that you can
[Page 163] start assigning permissions to users, groups, and roles.
[Page 163] Keep in mind that AWS managed policies might not grant least-privilege permissions for your
[Page 163] specific use cases because they're available for all AWS customers to use. We recommend that you
[Page 163] reduce permissions further by defining customer managed policies that are specific to your use
[Page 163] You cannot change the permissions defined in AWS managed policies. If AWS updates the
[Page 163] permissions defined in an AWS managed policy, the update affects all principal identities (users,
[Page 163] groups, and roles) that the policy is attached to. AWS is most likely to update an AWS managed
[Page 163] policy when a new AWS service is launched or new API operations become available for existing
[Page 163] For more information, see AWS managed policies in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 163] AWS managed policy: AWSGroundStationAgentInstancePolicy
[Page 163] You can attach the AWSGroundStationAgentInstancePolicy policy to your IAM identities.
[Page 163] This policy grants AWS Ground Station Agent permissions to your Amazon EC2 instance that allows
[Page 163] the instance to send and receive data during Ground Station contacts. All permissions in this policy
[Page 163] are from the Ground Station service.
[Page 163] This policy includes the following permissions.
[Page 163] • groundstation – Allows dataflow endpoint instances to call the Ground Station Agent APIs.

[Page 165] Use service-linked roles for Ground Station
[Page 165] AWS Ground Station uses AWS Identity and Access Management (IAM) service-linked roles. A
[Page 165] service-linked role is a unique type of IAM role that is linked directly to Ground Station. Service-
[Page 165] linked roles are predefined by Ground Station and include all the permissions that the service
[Page 165] requires to call other AWS services on your behalf.
[Page 165] A service-linked role makes setting up Ground Station easier because you don’t have to manually
[Page 165] add the necessary permissions. Ground Station defines the permissions of its service-linked roles,
[Page 165] and unless defined otherwise, only Ground Station can assume its roles. The defined permissions
[Page 165] Use service-linked roles 157
[Page 166] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 166] include the trust policy and the permissions policy, and that permissions policy cannot be attached
[Page 166] to any other IAM entity.
[Page 166] For information about other services that support service-linked roles, see AWS services that work
[Page 166] with IAM and look for the services that have Yes in the Service-linked roles column. Choose a Yes
[Page 166] with a link to view the service-linked role documentation for that service.
[Page 166] Service-linked role permissions for Ground Station
[Page 166] Ground Station uses the service-linked role named
[Page 166] AWSServiceRoleForGroundStationDataflowEndpointGroup – AWS GroundStation uses this
[Page 166] service-linked role to invoke EC2 to find public IPv4 addresses.
[Page 166] The AWSServiceRoleForGroundStationDataflowEndpointGroup service-linked role trusts the
[Page 166] following services to assume the role:
[Page 166] • groundstation.amazonaws.com
[Page 166] The role permissions policy named
[Page 166] AWSServiceRoleForGroundStationDataflowEndpointGroupPolicy allows Ground Station to
[Page 166] complete the following actions on the specified resources:
[Page 166] • Action: ec2:DescribeAddresses on all AWS resources (*)
[Page 166] Action allows Ground Station to list all IPs associated with EIPs.
[Page 166] • Action: ec2:DescribeNetworkInterfaces on all AWS resources (*)
[Page 166] Action allows Ground Station to get information on the network interfaces associated with EC2
[Page 166] You must configure permissions to allow an IAM entity (such as a user, group, or role) to create,
[Page 166] edit, or delete a service-linked role. For more information, see Service-linked role permissions in
[Page 166] Creating a service-linked role for Ground Station
[Page 166] You don't need to manually create a service-linked role. When you create a
[Page 166] DataflowEndpointGroup in the AWS CLI or the AWS API, Ground Station creates the service-linked
[Page 166] Service-linked role permissions for Ground Station 158
[Page 167] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 167] If you delete this service-linked role, and then need to create it again, you can use the same process
[Page 167] to recreate the role in your account. When you create a DataflowEndpointGroup, Ground Station
[Page 167] creates the service-linked role for you again.
[Page 167] You can also use the IAM console to create a service-linked role with the Data Delivery to
[Page 167] Amazon EC2 use case. In the AWS CLI or the AWS API, create a service-linked role with the
[Page 167] groundstation.amazonaws.com service name. For more information, see Creating a service-
[Page 167] linked role in the IAM User Guide. If you delete this service-linked role, you can use this same
[Page 167] process to create the role again.
[Page 167] Editing a service-linked role for Ground Station
[Page 167] Ground Station does not allow you to edit the
[Page 167] AWSServiceRoleForGroundStationDataflowEndpointGroup service-linked role. After you create
[Page 167] a service-linked role, you cannot change the name of the role because various entities might
[Page 167] reference the role. However, you can edit the description of the role using IAM. For more
[Page 167] information, see Editing a service-linked role in the IAM User Guide.
[Page 167] Deleting a service-linked role for Ground Station
[Page 167] If you no longer need to use a feature or service that requires a service-linked role, we recommend
[Page 167] that you delete that role. That way you don’t have an unused entity that is not actively monitored
[Page 167] You can delete a service-linked role only after first deleting the DataflowEndpointGroups using
[Page 167] the service-linked role. This protects you from inadvertently revoking permissions to your
[Page 167] DataflowEndpointGroups. If a service-linked role is used with multiple DataflowEndpointGroups,
[Page 167] you must delete all DataflowEndpointGroups that use the service-linked role before you can delete
[Page 167] If the Ground Station service is using the role when you try to delete the resources, then
[Page 167] the deletion might fail. If that happens, wait for a few minutes and try the operation again.
[Page 167] To delete Ground Station resources used by the
[Page 167] AWSServiceRoleForGroundStationDataflowEndpointGroup
[Page 167] • Delete DataflowEndpointGroups via the AWS CLI or the AWS API.

[Page 168] Data encryption at rest for AWS Ground Station
[Page 168] AWS Ground Station provides encryption by default to protect your sensitive data at rest using
[Page 168] AWS owned encryption keys.
[Page 168] • AWS owned keys - AWS Ground Station uses these keys by default to automatically encrypt
[Page 168] personal, directly identifiable data and ephemerides. You cannot view, manage, or use AWS-
[Page 168] owned keys, or audit their use; however, it is unnecessary to take any action or change programs
[Page 168] to protect the keys that encrypt data. For more information, see AWS-owned keys in the AWS
[Page 168] Key Management Service Developer Guide.
[Page 168] Encryption of data at rest by default helps by reducing the operational overhead and complexity
[Page 168] involved in protecting sensitive data. At the same time, it enables building secure applications that
[Page 168] meet strict encryption compliance, as well as regulatory requirements.
[Page 168] AWS Ground Station enforces encryption on all sensitive, at-rest, data, however, for some AWS
[Page 168] Ground Station resource, such as ephemerides, you can choose to use a customer managed key in
[Page 168] place of the default AWS managed keys.
[Page 168] Supported regions for Ground Station service-linked roles 160
[Page 169] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 169] • Customer managed keys -- AWS Ground Station supports the use of a symmetric customer
[Page 169] managed key that you create, own, and manage in place of the existing AWS owned encryption.
[Page 169] Because you have full control of this layer of encryption, you can perform such tasks as:
[Page 169] • Establishing and maintaining key policies
[Page 169] • Establishing and maintaining IAM policies and grants
[Page 169] • Enabling and disabling key policies
[Page 169] • Rotating key cryptographic material
[Page 169] • Creating key aliases
[Page 169] • Scheduling keys for deletion
[Page 169] For more information, see customer managed key in the AWS Key Management Service
[Page 169] The following table summarizes resources for which AWS Ground Station supports the use of
[Page 169] Customer Managed Keys
[Page 169] Data type AWS owned key encryption Customer managed key
[Page 169] encryption (Optional)
[Page 169] Ephemeris data used to Enabled Enabled
[Page 169] compute the trajectory of a
[Page 169] Azimuth elevation ephemeris Enabled Enabled
[Page 169] used to command antennas
[Page 169] AWS Ground Station automatically enables encryption at rest using AWS owned keys to
[Page 169] protect personally identifiable data at no charge. However, AWS KMS charges apply for
[Page 169] using a customer managed key. For more information about pricing, see the AWS Key
[Page 169] Management Service pricing.
[Page 169] Data encryption at rest for AWS Ground Station 161
[Page 170] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 170] For more information on AWS KMS, see the AWS Key Management Service Developer
[Page 170] For information specific to each resource type, see:
[Page 170] • Encryption at rest for TLE and OEM ephemeris data
[Page 170] • Encryption at rest for azimuth elevation ephemeris
[Page 170] Create a customer managed key
[Page 170] You can create a symmetric customer managed key by using the AWS Management Console, or the
[Page 170] To create a symmetric customer managed key
[Page 170] Follow the steps for creating symmetric customer managed key in the AWS Key Management
[Page 170] Service Developer Guide.
[Page 170] Key policies control access to your customer managed key. Every customer managed key must have
[Page 170] exactly one key policy, which contains statements that determine who can use the key and how
[Page 170] they can use it. When you create your customer managed key, you can specify a key policy. For
[Page 170] more information, see Managing access to customer managed keys in the AWS Key Management
[Page 170] Service Developer Guide.
[Page 170] To use your customer managed key with AWS Ground Station resources, you must configure
[Page 170] the key policy to grant appropriate permissions to the AWS Ground Station service. The specific
[Page 170] permissions and policy configuration depend on the type of resource you're encrypting:
[Page 170] • For TLE and OEM ephemeris data - See Encryption at rest for TLE and OEM ephemeris data for
[Page 170] specific key policy requirements and examples.
[Page 170] • For azimuth elevation ephemeris data - See Encryption at rest for azimuth elevation ephemeris
[Page 170] for specific key policy requirements and examples.
[Page 170] Create a customer managed key 162
[Page 171] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 171] The key policy configuration differs between ephemeris types. TLE and OEM ephemeris
[Page 171] data uses grants for key access, while azimuth elevation ephemeris uses direct key policy
[Page 171] permissions. Ensure you configure your key policy according to the specific resource type
[Page 171] For more information about specifying permissions in a policy and troubleshooting key access ,
[Page 171] see the AWS Key Management Service Developer Guide.
[Page 171] Specifying a customer managed key for AWS Ground Station
[Page 171] You can specify a customer managed key to encrypt the following resources:
[Page 171] • Ephemeris (TLE, OEM, and azimuth elevation)
[Page 171] When you create a resource, you can specify the data key by providing a kmsKeyArn
[Page 171] • kmsKeyArn - A key identifier for an AWS KMS customer managed key
[Page 171] AWS Ground Station encryption context
[Page 171] An encryption context is an optional set of key-value pairs that contain additional contextual
[Page 171] information about the data. AWS KMS uses the encryption context as additional authenticated
[Page 171] data to support authenticated encryption. When you include an encryption context in a request to
[Page 171] encrypt data, AWS KMS binds the encryption context to the encrypted data. To decrypt data, you
[Page 171] include the same encryption context in the request.
[Page 171] AWS Ground Station uses different encryption context depending on the resource being encrypted
[Page 171] and specifies a specific encryption context for each key grant created.
[Page 171] For resource-specific encryption context details, see:
[Page 171] • Encryption at rest for TLE and OEM ephemeris data
[Page 171] • Encryption at rest for azimuth elevation ephemeris
[Page 171] Specifying a customer managed key for AWS Ground Station 163
[Page 172] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 172] Encryption at rest for TLE and OEM ephemeris data
[Page 172] Key policy requirements for TLE and OEM ephemeris
[Page 172] To use a customer managed key with ephemeris data, your key policy must grant the following
[Page 172] permissions to the AWS Ground Station service:
[Page 172] • kms:CreateGrant - Creates an access grant on a customer managed key. Grants AWS Ground
[Page 172] Station access to perform grant operations on the customer managed key for reading and
[Page 172] storing encrypted data.
[Page 172] • kms:DescribeKey - Provides the customer managed key details to allow AWS Ground Station
[Page 172] to validate the key before attempting to use the provided key.
[Page 172] For more information about Using Grants, see the AWS Key Management Service Developer Guide.
[Page 172] IAM user permissions for creating ephemeris with customer managed keys
[Page 172] When AWS Ground Station uses a customer managed key in cryptographic operations, it acts on
[Page 172] behalf of the user who is creating the ephemeris resource.
[Page 172] To create an ephemeris resource using a customer managed key, a user must have permissions to
[Page 172] call the following operations on the customer managed key:
[Page 172] • kms:CreateGrant - Allows the user to create grants on the customer managed key on behalf
[Page 172] of AWS Ground Station.
[Page 172] • kms:DescribeKey - Allows the user to view the customer managed key details to validate the
[Page 172] You can specify these required permissions in a key policy, or in an IAM policy if the key policy
[Page 172] allows it. These permissions ensure that users can authorize AWS Ground Station to use the
[Page 172] customer managed key for encryption operations on their behalf.
[Page 172] How AWS Ground Station uses grants in AWS KMS for ephemeris
[Page 172] AWS Ground Station requires a key grant to use your customer-managed key.
[Page 172] When you upload an ephemeris encrypted with a customer managed key, AWS Ground Station
[Page 172] creates a key grant on your behalf by sending a CreateGrant request to AWS KMS. Grants in AWS
[Page 172] KMS are used to give AWS Ground Station access to a AWS KMS key in your account.
[Page 172] Encryption at rest for TLE and OEM ephemeris data 164
[Page 173] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 173] This allows AWS Ground Station to do the following:
[Page 173] • Call GenerateDataKey to generate an encrypted data key and store it, because the data key isn't
[Page 173] immediately used to encrypt.
[Page 173] • Call Decrypt to use the stored encrypted data key to access encrypted data.
[Page 173] • Call Encrypt to use the data key to encrypt data.
[Page 173] • Set up a retiring principal to allow the service to RetireGrant.
[Page 173] You can revoke access to the grant at any time. If you do, AWS Ground Station won't be able to
[Page 173] access any of the data encrypted by the customer managed key, which affects operations that are
[Page 173] dependent on that data. For example, if you remove a key grant from an ephemeris currently in
[Page 173] use for a contact then AWS Ground Station will be unable to use the provided ephemeris data for
[Page 173] pointing the antenna during the contact. This will cause the contact to end in a FAILED state.
[Page 173] Ephemeris encryption context
[Page 173] Key grants for encrypting ephemeris resources are bound to a specific satellite ARN.
[Page 173] "encryptionContext": {
[Page 173] "aws:groundstation:arn":
[Page 173] "arn:aws:groundstation::111122223333:satellite/00a770b0-082d-45a4-80ed-SAMPLE",
[Page 173] "arn:aws:s3:::customerephemerisbucket/0034abcd-12ab-34cd-56ef-123456SAMPLE"
[Page 173] Key grants are re-used for the same key-satellite pair.
[Page 173] Using encryption context for monitoring
[Page 173] When you use a symmetric customer managed key to encrypt your ephemerides, you can also use
[Page 173] the encryption context in audit records and logs to identify how the customer managed key is
[Page 173] being used. The encryption context also appears in logs generated by AWS CloudTrail or Amazon
[Page 173] Encryption at rest for TLE and OEM ephemeris data 165
[Page 174] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 174] Using encryption context to control access to your customer managed key
[Page 174] You can use the encryption context in key policies and IAM policies as conditions to control
[Page 174] access to your symmetric customer managed key. You can also use encryption context constraints
[Page 174] AWS Ground Station uses an encryption context constraint in grants to control access to the
[Page 174] customer managed key in your account or region. The grant constraint requires that the operations
[Page 174] that the grant allows use the specified encryption context.
[Page 174] The following are example key policy statements to grant access to a customer managed key for a
[Page 174] specific encryption context. The condition in this policy statement requires that the grants have an
[Page 174] encryption context constraint that specifies the encryption context.
[Page 174] The following example shows a key policy for ephemeris data bound to a satellite:
[Page 174] "Version":"2012-10-17",
[Page 174] "Sid": "Allow AWS Ground Station to Describe key",
[Page 174] "Service": "groundstation.us-east-1.amazonaws.com"
[Page 174] "Action": "kms:DescribeKey",
[Page 174] "Sid": "Allow AWS Ground Station to Create Grant on key",
[Page 174] "Service": "groundstation.us-east-1.amazonaws.com"
[Page 174] "Action": "kms:CreateGrant",
[Page 174] "kms:EncryptionContext:aws:groundstation:arn":
[Page 174] "arn:aws:groundstation::123456789012:satellite/satellite-id"

[Page 175] Monitoring your encryption keys for ephemeris
[Page 175] When you use an AWS Key Management Service customer managed key with your ephemeris
[Page 175] resources, you can use AWS CloudTrail or Amazon CloudWatch logs to track requests that AWS
[Page 175] Ground Station sends to AWS KMS. The following examples are CloudTrail events for CreateGrant,
[Page 175] GenerateDataKey, Decrypt, and DescribeKey to monitor AWS KMS operations called by AWS
[Page 175] Ground Station to access data encrypted by your customer managed key.
[Page 175] When you use an AWS KMS customer managed key to encrypt your ephemeris resources,
[Page 175] AWS Ground Station sends a CreateGrant request on your behalf to access the AWS KMS key
[Page 175] in your AWS account. The grant that AWS Ground Station creates is specific to the resource
[Page 175] associated with the AWS KMS customer managed key. In addition, AWS Ground Station uses the
[Page 175] RetireGrant operation to remove a grant when you delete a resource.

[Page 181] Key policy requirements for azimuth elevation ephemeris
[Page 181] To use a customer managed key with azimuth elevation ephemeris data, your key policy must grant
[Page 181] the following permissions to the AWS Ground Station service. Unlike TLE and OEM ephemeris data
[Page 181] which uses grants, azimuth elevation ephemeris uses direct key policy permissions for encryption
[Page 181] operations. This is a simpler method to manage the permissions of, and use your keys.
[Page 181] • kms:GenerateDataKey - Generates data keys for encrypting your azimuth elevation ephemeris
[Page 181] • kms:Decrypt - Decrypts the encrypted data keys when accessing your azimuth elevation
[Page 181] Example key policy granting AWS Ground Station access to a customer managed key
[Page 181] With azimuth elevation ephemeris, you must configure these permissions
[Page 181] directly in the key policy. The regional AWS Ground Station service principal (e.g.,
[Page 181] groundstation.region.amazonaws.com) must be granted these permissions in your
[Page 181] key policy statements. Without these statements added to the key policy AWS Ground
[Page 181] Station will be unable to store or access your custom azimuth elevation ephemeris.
[Page 181] "Version":"2012-10-17",
[Page 181] "Sid": "Allow AWS Ground Station to Describe key",
[Page 181] "Service": "groundstation.us-east-1.amazonaws.com"
[Page 181] "Action": "kms:DescribeKey",

[Page 182] IAM user permissions for creating azimuth elevation ephemeris with customer
[Page 182] When AWS Ground Station uses a customer managed key in cryptographic operations, it acts on
[Page 182] behalf of the user who is creating the azimuth elevation ephemeris resource.
[Page 182] To create an azimuth elevation ephemeris resource using a customer managed key, a user must
[Page 182] have permissions to call the following operations on the customer managed key:
[Page 182] • kms:GenerateDataKey - Allows the user to generate data keys for encrypting the azimuth
[Page 182] elevation ephemeris data.
[Page 182] • kms:Decrypt - Allows the user to decrypt data keys when accessing the azimuth elevation
[Page 182] • kms:DescribeKey - Allows the user to view the customer managed key details to validate the
[Page 182] You can specify these required permissions in a key policy, or in an IAM policy if the key policy
[Page 182] allows it. These permissions ensure that users can authorize AWS Ground Station to use the
[Page 182] customer managed key for encryption operations on their behalf.
[Page 182] How AWS Ground Station uses key policies for azimuth elevation ephemeris
[Page 182] When you provide azimuth elevation ephemeris data with a customer managed key, AWS Ground
[Page 182] Station uses key policies to access your encryption key. The permissions are granted directly to
[Page 182] Encryption at rest for azimuth elevation ephemeris 174
[Page 183] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 183] AWS Ground Station through key policy statements rather than through grants as with TLE or OEM
[Page 183] If you remove AWS Ground Station's access to the customer managed key, AWS Ground Station
[Page 183] won't be able to access any of the data encrypted by that key, which affects operations that are
[Page 183] dependent on that data. For example, if you remove key policy permissions for azimuth elevation
[Page 183] ephemeris currently in use for a contact, AWS Ground Station will be unable to use the provided
[Page 183] azimuth elevation data for commanding the antenna during the contact. This will cause the
[Page 183] contact to end in a FAILED state.
[Page 183] Azimuth elevation ephemeris encryption context
[Page 183] When AWS Ground Station uses your AWS KMS key to encrypt azimuth elevation ephemeris data,
[Page 183] the service specifies an encryption context. The encryption context is additional authenticated data
[Page 183] (AAD) that AWS KMS uses to ensure data integrity. When an encryption context is specified for an
[Page 183] encryption operation, the service must specify the same encryption context for the decryption
[Page 183] operation. Otherwise, decryption fails. The encryption context is also written to your CloudTrail
[Page 183] logs to help you understand why a given AWS KMS key was used. Your CloudTrail logs might
[Page 183] contain many entries describing the use of a AWS KMS key, but the encryption context in each log
[Page 183] entry can help you determine the reason for that particular use.
[Page 183] AWS Ground Station specifies the following encryption context when it performs cryptographic
[Page 183] operations with your customer managed key on an azimuth elevation ephemeris:
[Page 183] "encryptionContext": {
[Page 183] "aws:groundstation:ground-station-id": "Ohio 1",
[Page 183] "aws:groundstation:arn": "arn:aws:groundstation:us-
[Page 183] east-2:111122223333:ephemeris/00a770b0-082d-45a4-80ed-SAMPLE",
[Page 183] "aws:s3:arn": "arn:aws:s3:::customerephemerisbucket/00a770b0-082d-45a4-80ed-
[Page 183] The encryption context contains:
[Page 183] aws:groundstation:ground-station-id
[Page 183] The name of the ground station associated with the azimuth elevation ephemeris.

[Page 184] The ARN of the ephemeris resource.
[Page 184] The ARN of the ephemeris stored in Amazon S3.

[Page 189] Data encryption during transit for AWS Ground Station
[Page 189] AWS Ground Station provides encryption by default to protect your sensitive data during transit.
[Page 189] Data can be streamed between AWS Ground Station antenna locations and your Amazon EC2
[Page 189] instances in two ways, depending on the mission profile configuration.
[Page 189] • AWS Ground Station Agent
[Page 189] Data encryption during transit for AWS Ground Station 181
[Page 190] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 190] Each method of streaming data handles encrypting data in transit differently. The following
[Page 190] sections describe each method.
[Page 190] AWS Ground Station Agent streams
[Page 190] AWS Ground Station Agent encrypts its streams using customer managed AWS KMS keys. The AWS
[Page 190] Ground Station Agent running on your Amazon EC2 instance will automatically decrypt the stream
[Page 190] to provide decrypted data.
[Page 190] The AWS KMS key used for encrypting a stream is specified when creating a MissionProfile in
[Page 190] the streamsKmsKey parameter. All permissions granting AWS Ground Station access to the keys
[Page 190] are handled through the AWS KMS key policy attached to streamsKmsKey.
[Page 190] Dataflow endpoint streams
[Page 190] Dataflow endpoint streams are encrypted using Datagram Transport Layer Security (DTLS) . This is
[Page 190] done using self-signed certificates, and doesn't require additional configuration.
[Page 190] AWS Ground Station Agent streams 182
[Page 191] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 191] Example mission profile configurations
[Page 191] The examples provided show how to take a public broadcast satellite and create a mission profile
[Page 191] that supports it. The resulting templates are provided to help you take a public broadcast satellite
[Page 191] contact and to help you make decisions about your satellites.
[Page 191] • JPSS-1 - Public broadcast satellite (PBS) - Evaluation
[Page 191] • Public broadcast satellite utilizing Amazon S3 data delivery
[Page 191] • Public broadcast satellite utilizing a dataflow endpoint (narrowband)
[Page 191] • Public broadcast satellite utilizing a dataflow endpoint (demodulated and decoded)
[Page 191] • Public broadcast satellite utilizing AWS Ground Station Agent (wideband)
[Page 191] JPSS-1 - Public broadcast satellite (PBS) - Evaluation
[Page 191] This example section matches the Customer onboarding process overview. It provides a brief
[Page 191] compatibility analysis with AWS Ground Station and sets the stage for the specific examples that
[Page 191] As mentioned in the Public broadcast satellites section, you can utilize select satellites, or
[Page 191] communication paths of a satellite, that are publicly available. In this section we describe JPSS-1 in
[Page 191] the AWS Ground Station terms. For reference, we utilize the Joint Polar Satellite System 1 (JPSS-1)
[Page 191] Spacecraft High Rate Data (HRD) to Direct Broadcast Stations (DBS) Radio Frequency (RF) Interface
[Page 191] Control Document (ICD) to complete the example. Also, worth noting that JPSS-1 is associated to
[Page 191] The JPSS-1 satellite offers one uplink and three direct downlink communication paths, as seen in
[Page 191] Figure 1-1 of the ICD. Of these four communication paths, only the single High Rate Data (HRD)
[Page 191] downlink communication path is available for public consumption. Based on this, you'll see this
[Page 191] path will have much more specific data associated with it as well. The four paths are as follows:
[Page 191] • Command path (uplink) at 2067.27 MHz center frequency with a data rate of 2-128 kbps. This
[Page 191] path is not publicly accessible.
[Page 191] • Telemetry path (downlink) at 2247.5 MHz center frequency with a data rate of 1-524 kbps. This
[Page 191] path is not publicly accessible.
[Page 191] JPSS-1 - Public broadcast satellite (PBS) - Evaluation 183
[Page 192] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 192] • SMD path (downlink) at 26.7034 GHz center frequency with a data rate of 150-300 Mbps. This
[Page 192] path is not publicly accessible.
[Page 192] • The RF for the HRD path (downlink) at 7812 MHz center frequency with a data rate of 15 Mbps.
[Page 192] It has a 30 MHz bandwidth, and is right-hand-circular-polarized. When you onboard JPSS-1 with
[Page 192] AWS Ground Station, this is the communication path you get access to. This communication path
[Page 192] contains instrument science data, instrument engineering data, instrument telemetry data, and
[Page 192] real-time spacecraft housekeeping data.
[Page 192] As we compare the potential data paths, we see that the command (uplink), telemetry (downlink),
[Page 192] and HRD (downlink) paths meet the frequency, bandwidth, and multi-channel concurrent use
[Page 192] capabilities of AWS Ground Station. The SMD path is not compatible as the center frequency is out
[Page 192] of range of the existing receivers. For more information about the supported capabilities, see AWS
[Page 192] Ground Station Site Capabilities.
[Page 192] As the SMD path is not compatible with AWS Ground Station it will not be represented in
[Page 192] the example configurations.
[Page 192] As the command (uplink) and telemetry (downlink) paths are not defined in the ICD, nor are
[Page 192] they available for public use, the values provided when used are notional.
[Page 192] Public broadcast satellite utilizing Amazon S3 data delivery
[Page 192] This example builds off the analysis done in the JPSS-1 - Public broadcast satellite (PBS) -
[Page 192] Evaluation section of the user guide.
[Page 192] For this example, you'll need to assume a scenario -- you want to capture the HRD communication
[Page 192] path as digital intermediate frequency and store it for future batch processing. This saves off the
[Page 192] raw radio frequency (RF) in-phase quadrature (I/Q) samples after it has been digitized. Once the
[Page 192] data is in your Amazon S3 bucket, you can demodulate and decode the data using any software
[Page 192] you desire. See the MathWorks Tutorial for a detailed example of processing. After using this
[Page 192] Public broadcast satellite utilizing Amazon S3 data delivery 184
[Page 193] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 193] example, you may consider adding Amazon EC2 spot pricing components to process the data and
[Page 193] lower your overall processing costs.

[Page 208] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 208] The AquaSnppJpssTerraDigIF template includes the following additional resources:
[Page 208] • (Optional) CloudWatch Event Triggers - AWS Lambda Function that is triggered using
[Page 208] CloudWatch Events sent by AWS Ground Station before and after a contact. The AWS Lambda
[Page 208] Function will start and optionally stop your Receiver Instance.
[Page 208] • (Optional) EC2 Verification for Contacts - The option to use Lambda to set up a verification
[Page 208] system of your Amazon EC2 instance(s) for contacts with SNS notification. It is important to note
[Page 208] that this may incur charges depending on your current usage.
[Page 208] • Ground Station Amazon Machine Image Retrieval Lambda - The option to select what software
[Page 208] is installed in your instance and the AMI of your choice. The software options include DDX
[Page 208] 2.6.2 Only and DDX 2.6.2 with qRadio 3.6.0. These options will continue to expand as
[Page 208] additional software updates and features are released.
[Page 208] • Additional mission profiles - Mission profiles for additional public broadcast satellites (Aqua,
[Page 208] • Additional antenna-downlink configs - Antenna downlink configs for additional public
[Page 208] broadcast satellites (Aqua, SNPP, and Terra).
[Page 208] The values and parameters for the satellites in this template are already populated. These
[Page 208] parameters make it easy for you to use AWS Ground Station immediately with these satellites. You
[Page 208] do not need to configure your own values in order to use AWS Ground Station when using this
[Page 208] template. However, you can customize the values to make the template work for your use case.
[Page 208] Where do I receive my data?
[Page 208] The dataflow endpoint group is set up to use the receiver instance network interface that part
[Page 208] of the template creates. The receiver instance uses a dataflow endpoint application to receive
[Page 208] the data stream from AWS Ground Station on the port defined by the dataflow endpoint. Once
[Page 208] received, the data is available for consumption via UDP port 50000 on the loopback adapter of
[Page 208] the receiver instance. For more information about setting up a dataflow endpoint group, see
[Page 208] AWS::GroundStation::DataflowEndpointGroup.
[Page 208] Public broadcast satellite utilizing a dataflow endpoint
[Page 208] (demodulated and decoded)

[Page 208] Evaluation section of the user guide.

[Page 220] With the above resources, you now have the ability to schedule JPSS-1 contacts for synchronous
[Page 220] data delivery from any of your onboarded AWS Ground Station AWS Ground Station Locations.
[Page 220] The following is a complete CloudFormation template that includes all resources described in this
[Page 220] section combined into a single template that can be directly used in CloudFormation.
[Page 220] The CloudFormation template named AquaSnppJpss.yml is designed to give you quick access
[Page 220] to start receiving data for the Aqua, SNPP, and JPSS-1/NOAA-20 satellites. It contains an Amazon
[Page 220] EC2 instance and the required AWS Ground Station resources to schedule contacts and receive
[Page 220] demodulated and decoded direct broadcast data.
[Page 220] If Aqua, SNPP, JPSS-1/NOAA-20, and Terra are not onboarded to your account, see Onboard
[Page 220] You can access the template by accessing the customer onboarding Amazon S3 bucket
[Page 220] using valid AWS credentials. The links below use a regional Amazon S3 bucket. Change the
[Page 220] us-west-2 region code to represent the corresponding region of which you want to create
[Page 220] the CloudFormation stack in.
[Page 220] Additionally, the following instructions use YAML. However, the templates are available
[Page 220] in both YAML and JSON format. To use JSON, replace the .yml file extension with .json
[Page 220] when downloading the template.
[Page 220] To download the template using AWS CLI, use the following command:
[Page 220] aws s3 cp s3://groundstation-cloudformation-templates-us-west-2/AquaSnppJpss.yml .
[Page 220] You can view and download the template in the console by navigating to the following URL in your
[Page 220] https://s3.console.aws.amazon.com/s3/object/groundstation-cloudformation-templates-us-
[Page 220] west-2/AquaSnppJpss.yml
[Page 220] You can specify the template directly in CloudFormation using the following link:

[Page 221] What additional resources does the template define?
[Page 221] The AquaSnppJpss template includes the following additional resources:
[Page 221] • (Optional) CloudWatch Event Triggers - AWS Lambda Function that is triggered using
[Page 221] CloudWatch Events sent by AWS Ground Station before and after a contact. The AWS Lambda
[Page 221] Function will start and optionally stop your Receiver Instance.
[Page 221] • (Optional) EC2 Verification for Contacts - The option to use Lambda to set up a verification
[Page 221] system of your Amazon EC2 instance(s) for contacts with SNS notification. It is important to note
[Page 221] that this may incur charges depending on your current usage.
[Page 221] • Ground Station Amazon Machine Image Retrieval Lambda - The option to select what software
[Page 221] is installed in your instance and the AMI of your choice. The software options include DDX 2.6.2
[Page 221] Only and DDX 2.6.2 with qRadio 3.6.0. If you want to use Wideband DigIF Data Delivery
[Page 221] and the AWS Ground Station Agent, please see Public broadcast satellite utilizing AWS Ground
[Page 221] Station Agent (wideband). These options will continue to expand as additional software updates
[Page 221] and features are released.
[Page 221] • Additional mission profiles - Mission profiles for additional public broadcast satellites (Aqua,
[Page 221] • Additional antenna-downlink configs - Antenna downlink configs for additional public
[Page 221] broadcast satellites (Aqua, SNPP, and Terra).
[Page 221] The values and parameters for the satellites in this template are already populated. These
[Page 221] parameters make it easy for you to use AWS Ground Station immediately with these satellites. You
[Page 221] do not need to configure your own values in order to use AWS Ground Station when using this
[Page 221] template. However, you can customize the values to make the template work for your use case.

[Page 221] the data stream from AWS Ground Station on the port defined by the dataflow endpoint. Once

[Page 235] The following is a complete CloudFormation template that includes all resources described in this
[Page 235] section combined into a single template that can be directly used in CloudFormation.
[Page 235] The CloudFormation template named
[Page 235] DirectBroadcastSatelliteWbDigIfEc2DataDelivery.yml is designed to give you
[Page 235] quick access to start receiving digitized intermediate frequency (DigIF) data for the Aqua, SNPP,
[Page 235] JPSS-1/NOAA-20, and Terra satellites. It contains an Amazon EC2 instance and the required
[Page 235] CloudFormation resources to receive raw DigIF direct broadcast data using AWS Ground Station
[Page 235] If Aqua, SNPP, JPSS-1/NOAA-20, and Terra are not onboarded to your account, see Onboard
[Page 235] Putting it together 227
[Page 236] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 236] You can access the template by accessing the customer onboarding Amazon S3 bucket
[Page 236] using valid AWS credentials. The links below use a regional Amazon S3 bucket. Change the
[Page 236] us-west-2 region code to represent the corresponding region of which you want to create
[Page 236] the CloudFormation stack in.
[Page 236] Additionally, the following instructions use YAML. However, the templates are available
[Page 236] in both YAML and JSON format. To use JSON, replace the .yml file extension with .json
[Page 236] when downloading the template.
[Page 236] To download the template using AWS CLI, use the following command:
[Page 236] aws s3 cp s3://groundstation-cloudformation-templates-us-west-2/agent/ec2_delivery/
[Page 236] DirectBroadcastSatelliteWbDigIfEc2DataDelivery.yml .
[Page 236] You can view and download the template in the console by navigating to the following URL in your
[Page 236] https://s3.console.aws.amazon.com/s3/object/groundstation-cloudformation-templates-us-
[Page 236] west-2/agent/ec2_delivery/DirectBroadcastSatelliteWbDigIfEc2DataDelivery.yml
[Page 236] You can specify the template directly in CloudFormation using the following link:
[Page 236] https://groundstation-cloudformation-templates-us-west-2.s3.us-west-2.amazonaws.com/
[Page 236] agent/ec2_delivery/DirectBroadcastSatelliteWbDigIfEc2DataDelivery.yml
[Page 236] What additional resources does the template define?
[Page 236] The DirectBroadcastSatelliteWbDigIfEc2DataDelivery template includes the following
[Page 236] additional resources:
[Page 236] • Receiver Instance Elastic Network Interface - (Conditional) An elastic network interface is
[Page 236] created in the subnet specified by PublicSubnetId if provided. This is required if the receiver
[Page 236] instance is in a private subnet. The elastic network interface will be associated with the EIP and
[Page 236] attached to the receiver instance.
[Page 236] • Receiver Instance Elastic IP - An elastic IP that AWS Ground Station will connect to. This
[Page 236] attaches to the receiver instance or elastic network interface.
[Page 236] Putting it together 228
[Page 237] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 237] • One of the following Elastic IP associations:
[Page 237] • Receiver Instance to Elastic IP Association - The association of the Elastic IP to your receiver
[Page 237] instance, if PublicSubnetId is not specified. This requires that SubnetId reference a public
[Page 237] • Receiver Instance Elastic Network Interface to Elastic IP Association - The association of the
[Page 237] elastic IP to the receiver instance elastic network interface, if PublicSubnetId is specified.
[Page 237] • (Optional) CloudWatch Event Triggers - AWS Lambda Function that is triggered using
[Page 237] CloudWatch Events sent by AWS Ground Station before and after a contact. The AWS Lambda
[Page 237] Function will start and optionally stop your Receiver Instance.
[Page 237] • (Optional) Amazon EC2 Verification for Contacts - The option to use Lambda to set up a
[Page 237] verification system of your Amazon EC2 instance(s) for contacts with SNS notification. It is
[Page 237] important to note that this may incur charges depending on your current usage.
[Page 237] • Additional mission profiles - Mission profiles for additional public broadcast satellites (Aqua,
[Page 237] • Additional antenna-downlink configs - Antenna downlink configs for additional public
[Page 237] broadcast satellites (Aqua, SNPP, and Terra).
[Page 237] The values and parameters for the satellites in this template are already populated. These
[Page 237] parameters make it easy for you to use AWS Ground Station immediately with these satellites. You
[Page 237] do not need to configure your own values in order to use AWS Ground Station when using this
[Page 237] template. However, you can customize the values to make the template work for your use case.
[Page 237] Where do I receive my data?
[Page 237] The dataflow endpoint group is set up to use the receiver instance network interface
[Page 237] that part of the template creates. The receiver instance uses the AWS Ground Station
[Page 237] Agent to receive the data stream from AWS Ground Station on the port defined by the
[Page 237] dataflow endpoint. For more information about setting up a dataflow endpoint group, see
[Page 237] AWS::GroundStation::DataflowEndpointGroup. For more information about the AWS Ground
[Page 237] Station Agent, see What is the AWS Ground Station Agent?
[Page 237] Putting it together 229

[Page 238] Troubleshoot contacts that deliver data to Amazon EC2
[Page 238] If you are unable to successfully complete an AWS Ground Station contact, you'll need to verify
[Page 238] that your Amazon EC2 instance is running, verify that your dataflow endpoint application is
[Page 238] running, and verify that your dataflow endpoint application's stream is configured properly.
[Page 238] DataDefender (DDX) is an example of a dataflow endpoint application currently supported
[Page 238] by AWS Ground Station
[Page 238] The following procedures assume that an Amazon EC2 instance is already set up. To set up an
[Page 238] Amazon EC2 instance in AWS Ground Station, see Getting Started.
[Page 238] Step 1: Verify that your EC2 instance is running
[Page 238] The following procedure shows how to find your Amazon EC2 instance in the console and start it if
[Page 238] 1. Locate the Amazon EC2 instance that was used for the contact you are troubleshooting. Use
[Page 238] Troubleshoot contacts that deliver data to Amazon EC2 230
[Page 239] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 239] a. In your CloudFormation dashboard, select the stack that contains your Amazon EC2
[Page 239] b. Choose the Resources tab and locate your Amazon EC2 instance in the Logical ID column.
[Page 239] Verify that the instance is created in the Status column.
[Page 239] c. In the Physical ID column, choose the link for your Amazon EC2 instance. This will take
[Page 239] you to the Amazon EC2 management console.
[Page 239] 2. In the Amazon EC2 management console, ensure that your Amazon EC2 Instance State is
[Page 239] 3. If your instance is running, continue to the next step. If your instance is not running, start the
[Page 239] instance by using the following step:
[Page 239] • With your Amazon EC2 instance selected, choose Actions > Instance State > Start.
[Page 239] Step 2: Determine type of dataflow application used
[Page 239] If you are using the AWS Ground Station Agent for data delivery please redirect to section
[Page 239] Troubleshooting AWS Ground Station Agent. Otherwise, if you are using the DataDefender (DDX)
[Page 239] application continue to the section called “Step 3: Verify that dataflow application is running”.
[Page 239] Step 3: Verify that dataflow application is running
[Page 239] Verifying the status of DataDefender requires you to connect to your instance in Amazon EC2. For
[Page 239] more details on connecting to your instance, see Connect to your Linux instance.
[Page 239] The following procedure provides troubleshooting steps using commands in an SSH client.
[Page 239] 1. Open a terminal or command prompt and connect to your Amazon EC2 instance by using SSH.
[Page 239] Forward port 80 of the remote host in order to view the DataDefender web UI. The following
[Page 239] commands demonstrate how to use SSH to connect to an Amazon EC2 instance through a
[Page 239] bastion with port forwarding enabled.
[Page 239] You must replace <SSH KEY>, <BASTION HOST>, and <HOST> with your specific ssh
[Page 239] key, bastion host name, and Amazon EC2 instance host name.
[Page 239] Step 2: Determine type of dataflow application used 231
[Page 240] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 240] ssh -L 8080:localhost:80 -o ProxyCommand="C:\Windows\System32\OpenSSH\ssh.exe -o
[Page 240] \"ForwardAgent yes\" -W %h:%p -i \"<SSH KEY>\" ec2-user@<BASTION HOST>" -i "<SSH
[Page 240] KEY>" ec2-user@<HOST>
[Page 240] ssh -L 8080:localhost:80 -o ProxyCommand="ssh -A -o 'ForwardAgent yes' -W %h:%p -i
[Page 240] <SSH KEY> ec2-user@<BASTION HOST>" -i <SSH KEY> ec2-user@<HOST>
[Page 240] 2. Verify that DataDefender (also called DDX) is running by grepping (checking) for a running
[Page 240] process named ddx in the output. The command for grepping (checking) for a running process
[Page 240] and a successful example output is provided below.
[Page 240] [ec2-user@Receiver-Instance ~]$ ps –ef | grep ddx
[Page 240] Rtlogic 4977 1 10 Oct16 ? 2-00:22:14 /opt/rtlogic/ddx/
[Page 240] bin/ddx –m/opt/rtlogic/ddx/modules –p/opt/rtlogic/ddx/plugins –c/opt/rtlogic/
[Page 240] ddx/bin/ddx.xml –umask=077 –daemon –f installed=true –f security=true –f enable
[Page 240] Ec2-user 18787 18657 0 16:51 pts/0 00:00:00 grep –color=auto ddx
[Page 240] If DataDefender is running, skip to the section called “Step 4: Verify that your dataflow
[Page 240] application stream is configured” Otherwise, continue to the next step.
[Page 240] 3. Start DataDefender using the command show below.
[Page 240] sudo service rtlogic-ddx start
[Page 240] If DataDefender is running after using the command, skip to the section called “Step 4: Verify
[Page 240] that your dataflow application stream is configured” Otherwise, continue to the next step.
[Page 240] 4. Inspect the following files using the commands below to see if there were any errors while
[Page 240] installing and configuring DataDefender.
[Page 240] cat /var/log/user-data.log
[Page 240] cat /opt/aws/groundstation/.startup.out
[Page 240] Step 3: Verify that dataflow application is running 232
[Page 241] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 241] A common issue discovered when inspecting these files is that the Amazon VPC
[Page 241] that your Amazon EC2 instance is running in does not have access to Amazon S3 to
[Page 241] download the installation files. If you discover in your logs that this is the issue, check
[Page 241] your EC2 instance's Amazon VPC and security group settings to ensure they are not
[Page 241] blocking access to Amazon S3.
[Page 241] If DataDefender is running after checking your Amazon VPC settings, continue to the section
[Page 241] called “Step 4: Verify that your dataflow application stream is configured”. If the problem
[Page 241] persists, contact AWS Support and send your log files with a description of your issue.
[Page 241] Step 4: Verify that your dataflow application stream is configured
[Page 241] 1. In a web browser, access your DataDefender web user interface by entering the following
[Page 241] address in the address bar: localhost:8080. Then, press Enter.
[Page 241] 2. On the DataDefender dashboard, choose Go to Details.
[Page 241] 3. Select your stream from the list of streams, and choose Edit Stream.
[Page 241] 4. In the Stream Wizard dialog box, do the following:
[Page 241] a. In the WAN Transport pane, ensure WAN to LAN is selected for Stream Direction.
[Page 241] b. In the Port box, ensure the WAN port you have chosen for your dataflow endpoint group
[Page 241] is present. By default, this port is 55888. Then, choose Next.
[Page 241] Step 4: Verify that your dataflow application stream is configured 233
[Page 242] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 242] c. In the Local Endpoint pane, ensure that a valid port is present in the Port box. By default,
[Page 242] this port is 50000. This is the port on which you'll receive your data after DataDefender
[Page 242] has received it from the AWS Ground Station service. Then, choose Next.
[Page 242] d. Choose Finish in the remaining menu if you have changed any values. Otherwise, you can
[Page 242] cancel out of the Stream Wizard menu.
[Page 242] Step 4: Verify that your dataflow application stream is configured 234
[Page 243] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 243] You have now ensured that your Amazon EC2 instance and DataDefender are both running and
[Page 243] configured properly to receive data from AWS Ground Station. Continue to the section called “Step
[Page 243] 5: Ensure you have enough available IP addresses in your receiver instance(s) subnet”.
[Page 243] Step 5: Ensure you have enough available IP addresses in your receiver
[Page 243] The following procedure shows how to find the number of available IP addresses in an Amazon EC2
[Page 243] reciever instance in the console.
[Page 243] 1. For each Amazon EC2 receiver instance that was used for the contact you are troubleshooting.
[Page 243] Use the following steps:
[Page 243] a. In your CloudFormation dashboard, select the stack that contains your Amazon EC2
[Page 243] b. Choose the Resources tab and locate your Amazon EC2 instance in the Logical ID column.
[Page 243] Verify that the instance is created in the Status column.
[Page 243] c. In the Physical ID column, choose the link for your Amazon EC2 instance. This will take
[Page 243] you to the Amazon EC2 management console.
[Page 243] 2. In the Amazon EC2 management console, find and click the Subnet ID link in your Amazon
[Page 243] EC2 receiver instance's Instance Summary. This will take you to the corresponding Amazon
[Page 243] VPC management console.
[Page 243] 3. Select the matching subnet in the Amazon VPC management console and check the Details of
[Page 243] your subnet for Available IPv4 addresses. If this number is not at least as many as dataflow
[Page 243] endpoints that use this Amazon EC2 receiver instance do the following:
[Page 243] a. Update your CloudFormation template's corresponding subnet CidrBlock to be sized
[Page 243] correctly. For more details on subnet sizing see, Subnet CIDR blocks.
[Page 243] b. Redeploy your stack with your updated CloudFormation template.
[Page 243] If you continue to experience issues, contact AWS Support.
[Page 243] Troubleshoot FAILED contacts
[Page 243] A contact will have a terminal contact status of FAILED when AWS Ground Station detects an
[Page 243] issue with your resource configuration. The common use cases that can cause FAILED contacts are
[Page 243] provided below, along with steps to help troubleshoot.
[Page 243] Step 5: Ensure you have enough available IP addresses in your receiver instance(s) subnet 235
[Page 244] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 244] This guide is specifically for the FAILED contact status - and is not intended for other
[Page 244] failure statuses, such as AWS_FAILED, AWS_CANCELLED, or FAILED_TO_SCHEDULE. For
[Page 244] more information on contact statuses, see the section called “AWS Ground Station contact
[Page 244] Dataflow endpoint FAILED use cases
[Page 244] The following is the list of common use cases that can result in a FAILED contact status for
[Page 244] dataflow endpoint based dataflows:
[Page 244] • Dataflow endpoint never connects - The connection between AWS Ground Station Antenna and
[Page 244] your Dataflow Endpoint Group for one or more dataflows was never established.
[Page 244] • Dataflow endpoint connects late - The connection between AWS Ground Station Antenna and
[Page 244] your Dataflow Endpoint Group for one or more dataflows was established after the contact start
[Page 244] • Dataflow endpoint’s subnet is out of available IP addresses - AWS Ground Station’s data
[Page 244] delivery solution is not able to create an ENI in your private network due to not having any
[Page 244] available IP address in the reciever instance's subnet.
[Page 244] • Dataflow endpoint’s subnet is invalid - AWS Ground Station’s data delivery solution is not able
[Page 244] to create an ENI in your private network due to inability to access the provided subnet specified
[Page 244] in the Dataflow Endpoint Group.
[Page 244] For any dataflow endpoint failure cases, it is recommended to look into the following:
[Page 244] • Confirm the receiver Amazon EC2 instance was started successfully, prior to contact start time.
[Page 244] • Confirm the dataflow endpoint software was up and running during the contact.
[Page 244] • Ensure you have at least one available IP address per dataflow endpoint per receiver instance
[Page 244] • Ensure subnets associated to your Dataflow Endpoint Group, through dataflows configured in
[Page 244] Set up and configure Amazon VPC, remain active and available to AWS Ground Station.
[Page 244] See the section on Troubleshoot contacts that deliver data to Amazon EC2 for more specific
[Page 244] troubleshooting steps.
[Page 244] Dataflow endpoint FAILED use cases 236
[Page 245] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 245] AWS Ground Station Agent FAILED use cases
[Page 245] The following is the list of common use cases that can result in a FAILED contact status for Agent-
[Page 245] • AWS Ground Station Agent Never Reported Status - The Agent responsible for orchestrating
[Page 245] data delivery on your Dataflow Endpoint Group for one or more dataflows never successfully
[Page 245] reported status to AWS Ground Station. This status update should happen within a few seconds
[Page 245] of the contact end time.
[Page 245] • AWS Ground Station Agent Started Late - The Agent responsible for orchestrating data delivery
[Page 245] on your Dataflow Endpoint Group for one or more dataflows was started late, after the contact
[Page 245] For any AWS Ground Station Agent dataflow failure cases, it is recommended to look into the
[Page 245] • Confirm the receiver Amazon EC2 instance was started successfully, prior to contact start time.
[Page 245] • Confirm the Agent application was up and running at the start and during the contact.
[Page 245] • Confirm the Agent application and Amazon EC2 instance were not shut down within 15 seconds
[Page 245] of contact end. This provides the Agent sufficient time to report status to AWS Ground Station.
[Page 245] See the section on Troubleshoot contacts that deliver data to Amazon EC2 for more specific
[Page 245] troubleshooting steps.
[Page 245] Troubleshoot FAILED_TO_SCHEDULE contacts
[Page 245] A contact will end in a FAILED_TO_SCHEDULE state when AWS Ground Station detects an issue
[Page 245] either with your resource configuration or within the internal system. A contact that ends in a
[Page 245] FAILED_TO_SCHEDULE state will optionally provide an errorMessage for additional context. For
[Page 245] information about describing contacts, see the DescribeContact API.
[Page 245] The common use cases that can cause FAILED_TO_SCHEDULE contacts are provided below, along
[Page 245] with steps to help troubleshoot.

[Page 246] This guide is specifically for the FAILED_TO_SCHEDULE contact status - and is not intended
[Page 246] for other failure statuses, such as AWS_FAILED, AWS_CANCELLED, or FAILED. For more
[Page 246] information on contact statuses, see the section called “AWS Ground Station contact
[Page 246] The settings specified in your Antenna Downlink Demod Decode Config
[Page 246] The mission profile that was used to schedule this contact had an antenna-downlink-demod-
[Page 246] decode config that was not valid.
[Page 246] Previously existing AntennaDownlinkDemodDecode config
[Page 246] • If your antenna-downlink-demod-decode configs have recently been changed - roll back to a
[Page 246] previously working version before attempting to schedule.
[Page 246] • If this was an intentional change on an existing config, or a previously existing config
[Page 246] that is no longer successfully scheduling - follow the next step on how to onboard a new
[Page 246] AntennaDownlinkDemodDecode config.
[Page 246] Newly created AntennaDownlinkDemodDecode config
[Page 246] Contact AWS Ground Station directly to onboard your new config. Create a case with AWS Support
[Page 246] including the contactId that ended in the FAILED_TO_SCHEDULE state

[Page 247] Troubleshoot DataflowEndpointGroups not in a HEALTHY state
[Page 247] Listed below are the reasons your dataflow endpoint groups may not be in a HEALTHY state as well
[Page 247] as the appropriate corrective action to take.
[Page 247] • NO_REGISTERED_AGENT - Start your EC2 instance, which will register the agent. Note that you
[Page 247] must have a valid controller config file for this call to be successful. Refer to the Use AWS Ground
[Page 247] Station Agent for details on configuring that file.
[Page 247] • INVALID_IP_OWNERSHIP - Use the DeleteDataflowEndpointGroup API to delete the Dataflow
[Page 247] Endpoint Group, then use the CreateDataflowEndpointGroup API to recreate the Dataflow
[Page 247] Endpoint Group using IP addresses and ports that are associated with the EC2 instance.
[Page 247] • UNVERIFIED_IP_OWNERSHIP - IP address has not been validated yet. Validation occurs
[Page 247] periodically so this should resolve itself.
[Page 247] • NOT_AUTHORIZED_TO_CREATE_SLR - Account is not authorized to create the necessary Service-
[Page 247] Linked Role. Check the troubleshooting steps in Use service-linked roles for Ground Station
[Page 247] Troubleshoot invalid ephemerides
[Page 247] When you upload ephemeris data to AWS Ground Station, it goes through an asynchronous
[Page 247] validation workflow. If validation fails, the ephemeris status will change to INVALID. The error
[Page 247] messaging in the DescribeEphemeris response provides detailed information to help you identify
[Page 247] and resolve the issue.
[Page 247] Understanding ephemeris validation errors
[Page 247] When an ephemeris fails validation, the DescribeEphemeris API response includes two fields to
[Page 247] help diagnose the problem:
[Page 247] A machine-readable code identifying the specific validation error. This can be used for
[Page 247] programmatic error handling.
[Page 247] A human-readable description of the validation error with specific details about what went
[Page 247] wrong and guidance on how to fix it.
[Page 247] Troubleshoot DataflowEndpointGroups not in a HEALTHY state 239
[Page 248] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 248] Example DescribeEphemeris response for an invalid ephemeris:
[Page 248] "ephemerisId": "abc12345-6789-def0-1234-567890abcdef",
[Page 248] "name": "My Invalid Ephemeris",
[Page 248] "creationTime": 1620254718.765,
[Page 248] "invalidReason": "METADATA_INVALID",
[Page 248] "errorCode": "OBJECT_NAME_MISSING",
[Page 248] "errorMessage": "Metadata field missing: OBJECT_NAME",
[Page 248] "ephemerisData": "[...]"
[Page 248] Common validation errors for TLE ephemerides
[Page 248] The following are common validation errors encountered when uploading TLE ephemerides:
[Page 248] Mismatched satellite catalog number
[Page 248] Error: "The satellite catalog number present in the ephemeris does not match the associated
[Page 248] satellite's satellite catalog number"
[Page 248] Solution: Verify that the NORAD ID/satellite catalog number in your TLE lines matches the
[Page 248] satellite catalog number of your satellite. Use 00000 for satellites without an assigned catalog
[Page 248] Error: "The mean motion of the provided ephemeris differs too greatly from the most recent
[Page 248] Solution: Verify that your TLE data is correct and represents a valid orbit. Ground Station uses
[Page 248] Space-Track ephemerides as a reference during validation.
[Page 248] Common validation errors for OEM ephemerides
[Page 248] The following are common validation errors encountered when uploading OEM ephemerides:
[Page 248] Common validation errors for TLE ephemerides 240
[Page 249] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 249] Invalid reference frame
[Page 249] Error: "The REF_FRAME is not supported"
[Page 249] Solution: Update your OEM file to use one of the supported reference frames: EME2000 or
[Page 249] Missing required fields
[Page 249] Error: "Metadata field missing: INTERPOLATION"
[Page 249] Solution: Add the INTERPOLATION and INTERPOLATION_DEGREE fields to your OEM metadata
[Page 249] section. These are required for AWS Ground Station to generate accurate antenna pointing
[Page 249] Unsupported time system
[Page 249] Error: "The TIME_SYSTEM is not supported"
[Page 249] Solution: Ensure your OEM file uses UTC as the time system.
[Page 249] Unsupported OEM version
[Page 249] Error: "The CCSDS_OEM_VERS is not supported"
[Page 249] Solution: Ensure your OEM file uses CCSDS OEM version 2.0.
[Page 249] Common validation errors for azimuth elevation ephemerides
[Page 249] The following are common validation errors encountered when uploading azimuth elevation
[Page 249] Missing azimuth/elevation data
[Page 249] Error: "No TimeAzEl fields were present in at least one AzElSegment"
[Page 249] Solution: Ensure each segment in your azimuth elevation data contains at least one time-tagged
[Page 249] azimuth/elevation pair.
[Page 249] Invalid azimuth angle range (degrees)
[Page 249] Error: "AzEl az must be greater than or equal to -180 and less or equal to 360 degrees"
[Page 249] Solution: Verify that azimuth angles are within [-180, 360] degrees.
[Page 249] Common validation errors for azimuth elevation ephemerides 241
[Page 250] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 250] Invalid elevation angle range (degrees)
[Page 250] Error: "AzEl el must be greater than or equal to -90 and less than or equal to 90 degrees"
[Page 250] Solution: Verify that elevation angles are within [-90, 90] degrees.
[Page 250] Invalid azimuth angle range (radians)
[Page 250] Error: "AzEl az must be greater than or equal to -pi and less than or equal to 2pi radians"
[Page 250] Solution: Verify that azimuth angles are within [-π, 2π] radians.
[Page 250] Invalid elevation angle range (radians)
[Page 250] Error: "AzEl el must be greater than or equal to -pi/2 and less than or equal to pi/2 radians"
[Page 250] Solution: Verify that elevation angles are within [-π/2, π/2] radians.
[Page 250] Non-monotonic time values
[Page 250] Error: "The TimeAzEl items within a AzElSegment must be temporally in order"
[Page 250] Solution: Ensure that the time values in each segment are strictly increasing.
[Page 250] Segments out of order
[Page 250] Error: "AzElSegments must be temporally in order"
[Page 250] Solution: Ensure that segments are arranged in chronological order.
[Page 250] Error: "The time range of at least one segment overlaps with other segment time ranges"
[Page 250] Solution: Ensure that each segment has a unique, non-overlapping time range. The endTime of
[Page 250] one segment should not exceed the startTime of the next segment.
[Page 250] Troubleshooting steps
[Page 250] If your ephemeris fails validation, follow these steps to resolve the issue:
[Page 250] 1. Call DescribeEphemeris with your ephemeris ID to retrieve the errorCode and errorMessage.
[Page 250] 2. Review the error message for specific details about what validation check failed.
[Page 250] Troubleshooting steps 242
[Page 251] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 251] 3. Correct the identified issues in your ephemeris data.
[Page 251] 4. Upload a new ephemeris with the corrected data using CreateEphemeris.
[Page 251] 5. Monitor the new ephemeris status until it reaches ENABLED state.
[Page 251] 6. Delete the invalid ephemeris using DeleteEphemeris if it's no longer needed.

[Page 255] Troubleshoot contacts that received no data
[Page 255] It is possible for a contact to appear successful, but still did not receive any data. This may mean
[Page 255] that you receive PCAP files that are empty, or no PCAP files at all if you are using S3 data delivery.
[Page 255] This can happen for a number of reasons. The following discusses some of the causes, and how to
[Page 255] Incorrect downlink config
[Page 255] Each contact that receives data from a satellite will have an associated Antenna Downlink Config
[Page 255] or Antenna Downlink Demod Decode Config . If the configuration specified does not agree with
[Page 255] the signal being transmitted by a satellite, AWS Ground Station will not be able to receive the
[Page 255] transmitted signal. This will result in no data being received by AWS Ground Station.
[Page 255] To fix this, please verify that the configs you are using agree with the signal being transmitted
[Page 255] by your satellite. For example, verify that you've set the correct center frequency, bandwidth,
[Page 255] polarization, and if needed, demodulation and decoding parameters.
[Page 255] There are times that a satellite may perform a maneuver which temporarily disables some of its
[Page 255] communication systems. The maneuver may also significantly change the location of the satellite
[Page 255] in the sky. AWS Ground Station will not be able to receive a signal from a satellite that is not
[Page 255] transmitting a signal, or if the ephemeris being used causes the AWS Ground Station antenna to
[Page 255] point at a location in the sky where the satellite is not present.
[Page 255] Troubleshoot contacts that received no data 247
[Page 256] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 256] If you are trying to communicate with a public broadcast satellite operated by NOAA, you may be
[Page 256] able to find a message describing an outage or maneuver on the NOAA Satellite Alert Messages
[Page 256] page. The message may include a timeline of when data transmission is expected to resume, or this
[Page 256] may be posted in a subsequent message.
[Page 256] If you are communicating with your own satellites, it's your responsibility to understand your
[Page 256] satellite operations, and how this might impact communicating with AWS Ground Station. If you
[Page 256] are performing a maneuver that will impact the satellite trajectory, this may include providing
[Page 256] updated custom ephemeris data. For more information on providing custom ephemeris data, see
[Page 256] Understand how AWS Ground Station uses ephemerides.
[Page 256] AWS Ground Station outage
[Page 256] If AWS Ground Station causes a contact to fail, or cancels it, AWS Ground Station will set the
[Page 256] contact status to AWS_FAILED, or AWS_CANCELLED. For more information on contact lifecycle,
[Page 256] see Understand contact lifecycle. In some cases, AWS Ground Station may have a failure that
[Page 256] prevents data from being delivered to your account, but doesn't result in the contact being in an
[Page 256] AWS_FAILED or AWS_CANCELLED status. When this happens, AWS Ground Station should post an
[Page 256] account-specific event to your AWS Health dashboard. For more information about the AWS Health
[Page 256] dashboard, see AWS Health User Guide .
[Page 256] AWS Ground Station outage 248
[Page 257] AWS Ground Station User Guide
[Page 257] You can view the supported regions, their associated endpoints, and quotas at AWS Ground Station
[Page 257] endpoints and quotas.
[Page 257] You can use the Service Quotas console, the AWS API and the AWS CLI to request quota increases,
